SATURDAY, JULY 2, 1892.
The Queensland Electoral Bill.
The Electoral Bill, which the Griffilwraith
Ministry has piloted through the Queens-
land Assembly by a vast majority, is nearly,
though not quite, the last of a series of mea-
sures whose seeming purpose it is to make
Samuel Griffith the perpetual Premier
and Dictator of the North. For apparently
it is the ambition of Griffith to be in
Queensland what Lopez and Franco a were
in Paraguay or Juarez in Mexico—a species
of irremovable potentate ruling over a free
nation, which, being interpreted, means a
nation that is free to vote for Griffith. The
Queensland Premier has always regarded
himself as more than a man and a shade less
than a god ; he has posed, ever since his first
appearance in politics, as something different
from those ordinary, makeshift politicians
who come and go and are presently forgotten
—he has struck a perpetual attitude as the
heaven-sent saviour of his country—as a
statesman whose reign would only be
terminated by death or voluntary resigna-
tion. The idea that the country which had
once possessed the inestimable blessing of
being ruled by Samuel Griffith would ever
voluntarily dispense with him lay altogether
outside his original calculations. In the
early part of his career he started out to
play the part of the King of the Poor, and
to reign in the hearts of the people by
virtue of certain weak and fuzzy maun-
derings about Socialism, human equality,
and the rights of mankind in general. There
seems to be every reason to believe that
Griffith, by reason of his theories and his
ill-digested platitudes, firmly believed him-
self at one time to be the father and bene-
factor of his country ; he put none of his
theories into execution nor did he attempt
to do so, but he was vaguely convinced that
Queensland owed him a debt of undying
gratitude merely because he possessed
shadowy yearnings after something which he
imagined to be progress. And when a
general election came,and MTlwraith swept
the country on his short-lived Nationalist
programme, Griffith felt as Jove might
have donc.had ho been kicked by a helot,
and believed, in his diseased and semi-crazed
vanity, that he was the victim of a monstrous
act of ingratitude because the nation which
he had blessed with a few badly-written and
incomprehensible essays had deserted him,
and his faith in humanity flickered out there
and then.
* # #
rherefore, when he came back to power,
be abandoned his role as the King of the
Poor, and proceeded to take a systematic
and personal vengeance on the people whose
crime it was that they had failed to appre-
ciate him at his own insane valuation. His
belief in the divine right of Griffith was
unimpaired, and those who did not believe
in that divine right he regarded much as the
Bourbons or the Valois did those who
failed to appreciate the beauties of their
system of government in the days of abso-
lutism. The central axiom of his faith is
that Queensland exists to furnish fame and
a permanent billet for that Griffith who is
only one degree lower than the angels, and
having learnt by experience that the nation
cannot be trusted to do its duty and vote
solid for him on all occasions, his policy is
now to systematically disfranchise every in-
dividual and every class which shows an
inclination to vote against him. A much
greater man, one Benito Juarez, when his
country failed to appreciate his abilities by
electing him for a second term, solved the
difficulty by the simple process of taking no
notice of his successor; - his term expired,
and he peacefully remained in office, and
when the people’s representative arrived to
take over the position, he merely froze him
with his eye, and declined to be aware of
his existence. Except for Griffith’s utter
lack of personal courage, he and Juarez
would be very much alike in their policy.
* * *
The new Electoral Bill is one more item
in that great scheme whereby nobody except
the supporters of Griffith will be allowed
to vote in Queensland, and anybody who
doesn’t vote for Griffith shall be disfran-
chised. It consists, to all intents and pur-
poses, of two clauses, which read as follows
Every claim made by a person to have his name inserted
ill an electoral roll must set forth the age and occupation
of the claimant, and must be declared by the applicant
before, and attested by a justice of the peace.
Any justice who signs any such certificate without per-
sonal knou’ledffe or full inquiry , for which purpose he
may require the applicant to depose to the fads upon
oath, shall he liable on summary conviction to a penalty
not exceeding fifty pounds, and on such conviction shail
be incapable of being or acting as a justice, or of being
registered as an elector or voting at any parliamentary
election for the period of five years from the date of the
conviction.
Under the new order of things in Queens-
land, therefore, no one shall have a vote
unless he can find a Justice of the Peace
who is able and willing to swear, upon his
own personal knowledge, to his identity.
Therefore, all the thousands of people,
mostly workmen, who are not acquainted
with any Justice of the Peace, are politically
abolished in one act. And for the rest of
the male population the case stands some-
what as follows.
• « *
In Queensland, with hardly an exception,
the J’s.P. are Conservatives of the old,
aggravated type, and the great majority are
members of the bogus-Patriotic League,
which supports Griffith, and the Kanaka
policy, and the land-grant railway Jobs,
and the interests of property generally.
Griffith, in fact, is the Patriotic League—
so long as he continues on his present course
—and the Patriotic League is Griffith, and
the J’s.P. are both of them under another
style and designation. Therefore, the demo-
crat, no matter how good his qualification,
who desires a vote, must apply, in the first
place, to one of the boss members of the
League—which is formed for the express
purpose of preventing him having a vote—
to attest his claim. If that individual
doesn’t know him he will refuse to sign the
necessary document ; if he does know him
he can refuse all the same. There is
abundant provision in the Act to suppress
the J.P. who signs a certificate that is
incorrect ; there is absolutely nothing about
the J.P. who refuses to sign the one that is
correct. The “ Patriotic ” League possesses
a tolerably accurate list of every democratic
voter in each electorate, and such an indivi-
dual will probably find hardly one Justice of
the Peace, from Torres Straits to the Border,
who will place him on the roll. If he does
find one, however, it is tolerably certain
that the J.P. in question will presently get
into trouble. The penal clause is very wide
and comprehensive, and if, by any chance,
the few Justices who are not the servants
and agents of the Patriotic League should
pass a sufficient number of voters to imperil
the Griffilwraith majority in any elec-
torate, it should be easy to get a conviction
against them and secure their abolition. The
number of persons that a J.P. can be per-
sonally acquainted with is limited, and he
cannot personally enquire into the cases of
more than a very small number, even if he
gives his whole time to the work. More-
over the new qualification is so restricted,
and so full of man-traps, spring-guns, and
pitfalls that no Justice, however careful, can
long avoid mistakes. Therefore, should any
official, or any score of officials, exhibit a
tendency to fill the roll with democratic
voters they will presently be entrapped into
signing certificates for some of the informers
of the Patriotic League, and then they will be
convicted before a bench filled with J’s.P.
who are members of the League, and thus
removed out of the way. The item which
provides for summary conviction is a note-
worthy feature in the scheme. A matter of
such importance, it might naturally be sup-
posed, would be relegated to the Supreme
Court, but jurisdiction is strictly reserved for
a tribunal where the Patriotic League runs
the entire show. And the League, as already
mentioned, is practically Griffith himself.
Under the new Electoral Act Griffith,
through his agents, has the sole power of
putting his own voters on the roll and keep-
ing all other voters off; he is the court which
sits in judgment; he is the prosecutor, the
judge, and the jury, and, in effect, he is the
whole electoral-roll in himself. In a word,
Griffith is to be Queensland and Queens-
land is to be Griffith. That is what the
whole thing amounts to.
# » «
Also the Upper House —by virtue of his
many nominations to that petrified institu-
tion—is mostly Griffith, and its assent to
the Electoral Bill is therefore almost a
matter of certainty. At such a time as this
the evils of the nominee system become most
conspicuous. When the country, in some
moment of aberration, elects an Assembly
with a bigoted Tory majority, that House at
once proceeds upon a course of retrograde
legislation which is enthusiastically endorsed
by the Council in the interests of Property ;
but when a democratic Assembly succeeds
to power, and endeavours to undo the
evil that has been accomplished, the
Upper House inevitably blocks the way.
Therefore, if the people of Queensland
should, by something approaching a miracle,
succeed in returning a Liberal majority at
the next election, the Electoral Bill will still
continue in force, unless the Council can be
scared into submission by the fear of actual
violence. The only variety in the legisla-
tive programme consists in the fact that the
great mass of the people have sometimes
both Houses to contend with and sometimes
only one. And now that the apostate
Griffith has a permanent lien upon the
Upper House, and has taken measures to
make himself sole proprietor of the Lower,
there is only one thing more to be done.
That is to give the two branches of the
Legislature equal authority, so that if, by
any unforeseen contingency, some future
Assembly should prove hostile, he can rest
his authority upon the support of the Coun-
cil, and put the people’s representatives
altogether at defiance, and run the country
by himself. It is not impossible that he may
make the attempt, for his madness becomes
every day more evident. The autocrat who
threatened, on his own single responsibility,
to “ exclude ” a Labour member from the
House because he objected to the Govern-
ment policy, and ■who accused the Labour
party of advocating the murder of their
political opponents, assuredly has high ambi-
tions to become a Dictator of the Lopez,
or Guzman Blanco, or Santa Anna order
if he only had the courage necessary for
such a role. Still, however, there is one
difficulty in his way which may, sooner or
later, prove fatal. The Electoral Bill, if his
subordinate agents dare to carry out its pro-
visions effectually, will make Griffith
Dictator of Queensland. But MTlwraith
sits on his shoulders like an Old Financier
of the Sea, and by-and-bye, in all pro-
bability, they will fall asunder through both
desiring supreme authority, and the Patriotic
League will have to choose between them.
If MHlw’raith comes uppermost then
Griffith will be sent into comparative
obscurity, and after that MHlwraith him-
self will probably die of apoplexy, and chaos
will follow. And if Griffith wins, the
chances are that M‘llwraith will turn his
coat once more, and stump the country with
a frantic policy of broken heads and flaming
houses, and thereupon Griffith will gather
up his legal blue bag and fly for his life.
There is a grave yawning for every Dictator,
and the one for the Queensland Premier is
already dug.
Australia at Chicago Exhibition.
If the New South Wales Government were to
accept D ires’ suggestion and withdraw its
application for space at Chicago Exhibition that
great show would be never one penny the worse,
But the province would be many thousands of
pounds the better, and at present it has cer-
tainly no thousands to throw away. For, with
the solitary exception of wool, Australia has
nothing to show the Yankees in which they are
interested, and has as much chance of opening
up a trade with them in any other commodity
as of transacting business with the man in the
moon-
m. „ TT .. . a , , * * , ,
the United States form a complete world,
mdependent entirely of the rest of mankind,
I lie republic has bo millions of people who grow'
everything, make everything, and possess
everything that humanity wants, as well as
many things wluca it never knew it wanted,
Every mineral that Australians have and all
the others that they have not are found within
United states territory and, therefore, the
collection or mineral exhibits upon which New
south Wales has spent so much money can only
serve to remind Americans of their special
superiority in this particular. As with minerals
so with timber. It is gravely asserted that
a thousand pounds has been spent on specimen
timbers to be exhibited at Chicago. In that
case the money has been thrown away in a
foolish exhibition of the nakedness of the
land, fhc Americans have, it is true, used
their glorious forests tastefully and have
denuded thousands of square miles of their
magnificent timber. But they have ample
supplies for centimes still remaining. There is
a larger quantity and greater variety of timber
within a belt of oO miles around Puget Sound
than the entire Australian Continent can show,
Indeed, with the exception of the travelling
commissioner, his clerks, servants, and
hangers-on, no one in the world can hv
possibility reap the slightest benefit from
expenditure of Australian money U nnn .
representation at Chicago Exhibition. ™ ■
** , ‘
It might be just as well for the 740
for space in the New South Wales couiUn , u S
this matter to heart and_ consider it narrowly
What good can they obtain by sending exhibit;
to Chicago beyond the gratification 0 f
vanity ? The Americans have firnt
wisely) determined not to buy one single m S
factured article which they can make at home'
They have purposely increased their tariff7A
prohibitive rates and have • thus struck so
deadly a blow' at Lritish exports that the shin
owners of the United Kingdom are gravelv
meditating the abandonment of the America
carrying-trade, simply because they have no
longer anything to carry to America. Should
tins course be adopted, American prod,mo
which the British buy yearly to thereof
about a hundred millions sterling, will | nvo fk
bear double freight, but that will not daunt the
Americans. The Briton will still chop ~n
American cattle with American knives and
hatchets, will still make American wheat into
English bread by the aid of American labour
saving machinery, will still build his houses of
American timber with American tools--the
latter pursuit being not entirely unknown in
Australia—and will still light them with Ameri-
can kerosene when finished. But he will not
be able to conduct his trade with Cousin
Jonathan by any system of barter. He will
have to plank down the coin.
* # •
Considering that there are thousands of men
out of work in Sydney, and thousands more in
Melbourne, Adelaide and Brisbane, and that
rigorous retrenchment is the recognised order
of the day all over Australia, it really seems
as though, it would be better to spend
the money Australian courts would cost in
local works, which would give bread to
the hungry, rather than upon the travelling
expenses of a parcel of honourable Commis-
sioners and their honourable flunkeys. If
Americans want Australian wool they will send
to Australia to buy it, or go to London to do so,
or perhaps invite direct shipments as the
Belgians did some years ago. But the Honour-
able Doctor Rex wick will haveno influence in the
matter, nor will the 740 N.S. W. exhibitors who
have applied for 200,000 feet of space, which is
nearly twice as much as the American authori-
ties feel inclined to grant, be of any service.
The wool-buyers, of whom there may be fifty,'
will take their own way and will not trouble
themselves about the Chicago exhibition. And
the best thing Australia can do is to leave it
alone also, and to devote to some useful purpose
of the poor the money which would have been
spent in useless extravagance by unnecessary
representatives.
How to Keep Out Smallpox.
Tji ere are many hundreds of Australian
medical men \\ ho have never seen smallpox
and would not know it if they did see it.
There are many scores of thousands of Austra-
lians who look upon the disease as extinct, who
regard it much as they do the plague or the
Black Death, and chafe mightily at the pre-
cautions which are adopted to prevent its
entrance or to stamp it out when it manages to
creep within our lines. This it often does, and
will do more frequently as the speed of steamers
increases and voyages are shorter. When it
does come we scream and sw'ear and hunt down
the victim and quarantine him or her, rather
brutally now and then, and sometimes pay
heavy compensation when we have nabbed
the wrong man, and then sit down
and plume ourselves on the vigorous
measures by which we “stamped out” the
enemy. But, excepting in our moments of
panic, we can hardly be brought to believe in
the enemy’s existence. We affect to think—a
good many of us—that the long-continued
ravages of smallpox were merely due to the
defective sanitary arrangements of our fore-
fathers and to the crowded condition of their
cities ; and we treat it as something that will
disappear as mankind learns better how to live.
So, for that matter, will typhoid, consumption,
and insanity, but just now it cannot be too
clearly understood that our partial immunity is
due to conditions which are rapidly disappear-
ing. Smallpox is always raging in Africa,
where overcrowding, at any rate, is not a rule
of life. It is rampant in China, and sovereign
in Ceylon, whence, indeed, comes our most
recent danger, and it is always hovering about
those islands of the southern archipelago whence
the Kanakas are presently to be brought into
Queensland to cultivate godliness and the
sugarcane.
« # *
Smallpox is one of the many gifts which the
Holy Land conferred upon the Crusaders who
took it back with them to Europe where it
reigned unchecked and scattered death broad-
cast for more than 700 years. Indeed, even
after Jenner discovered, in 1799, a counteract-
ing disorder, cow-pox, which, up. to this time,
has proved the best preventive, it took 50 years
hard fighting to reduce smallpox within
manageable limits in England alone. It is only
within the lifetime of the present generation
that British medical men have felt themselves
fairly able to cope with the malady or that the
sight of a pock-marked person has ceased to be
common in the London streets. Even yet con-
signments of the disorder come regularly to
that city from Smyrna and Aleppo, from Con-
stantinople and Cairo ; and no man who boards
a sailing ship on its arrival from the Levant
can be sure that he will not catch it himself or
convey it to his family. And as small-pox is
the most contagious of all febrile diseases, the
most dangerous, the most distressing, the most
debilitating and the most disgusting, it
can be easily understood that it is by no means
a welcome or desirable visitor. It makes the
victim an object of horror to those who love
him best, and of dread and loathing to those
who are otherwise always sympathetic and
helpful. It leaves behind it an ineffaceable
outward disfigurement, and an unconquerable
inward weakness, so that, in nine cases nut of
ten, the individual who happens to survive a


. seizure'finds himself unable to decide
' hether it would not, on the whole, have been
Tetter for him had he gone quietly off to limbo
Then he was first attacked.
# * #
Absolute and perfect isolation is still the
jy means of preventing the disease from
decimating the community in which it appears,
£ . e there is no moment, from that in
which the sufferer receives the contagion,
U 1 that in which he is pronounced
■fectly W ell, when he may not communi-
cate it to anybody about him. He infects the
■fir he breathes, the clothes he wears, the fur-
niture he uses, the house he inhabits, the wind
(hat passes over him, and the ground he treads
upon. If bis own case is a light one the chances
*g (hat he will communicate a heavier and
more virulent form of his ailment to any recep-
tive subject, and the result is that no one comes
within hail of him who can possibly keep away,
jf by chance it breaks out in a crowded London
court or alley inhabited by the very poor, there
is nothing to be done but to imprison the
wretched people in their miserable homes
until it has passed away, and the attend-
ants have to share the imprisonment and
take their chances. Those who have been suc-
cessfully and recently vaccinated or have them-
selves suffered from the disease escape, but
even medical science can do little for the
sufferer avlio is once infected. If the terrible
irritation can be allayed, the air passages kept
openandthestrength maintained, the victim may
live through it, and to these ends the doctors
address their utmost skill, very often in vain,
since, after the period of incubation is over and
the nature of the attack is unmistakeable, there
are still from fourteen to twenty-one days to be
passed before the crisis arrives and the balance
inclines towards life or death.
• # #
If we wish to keep smallpox out of Australia
we must tight it at the outports. Our first
quarantine station should not be in Port Pljillip
uor at Port Jackson, but at Colombo. Before
the Australian liner starts from that port, the
strictest medical investigation should be made,
in order that no person may embark who has
placed himself in danger. There is no
clanger at present of persons who come more
than a fortnight’s journey bringing infection
without the knowledge of the medical in-
spectors. A man who left England with the
seeds of the disease in his constitution would
be in a fair way to convalescence before he
could arrive here if he lived to complete the
voyage. It is the people who come from
infected places within a fortnight’s sail of our
shores we have to regard with suspicion, and
there ought not to be much difficulty in arrang-
ing for proper supervision of those persons
before they begin their journey.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
“Poor Jack!”
Some further revelations in the case of the
sailors of the British Commodore—who were
recently tried in Sydney by that eccentric
autocrat, Magistrate Lee —afford another un-
wholesome advertisement for Australian justice
as it is shovelled out by the stipendiary “beak”
of the period. Several weeks ago the crew of
this vessel were brought up on the elastic
charge of “disobedience,” they having inti-
mated—in effect—that they would sooner go to
gaol than take their chance on board the British
Commodore, which they alleged to be a total
Avreck aloft, and unfit to proceed to sea. As a
previous crew had been in trouble, also for
“ disobedience,” it might have occurred to the
Court that there must be something rotten in
the state of the vessel when so many sailors
were anxious to get out of her. Nevertheless,
Magistrate Lee didn’t even adjourn the case in
order to get independent evidence as to the
condition of the alleged coffin-ship ; he simply
listened to the statements of the captain, who
Avas an interested party, and having got his
single, bald declaration against the unanimous
testimony of the sailors, he sentenced the latter
to eight weeks’ imprisonment. He also went
out of his way to assure them that it didn’t
matter even though the press took up their
case, as they would do their two months
in gaol whatever happened. Then, hav-
ing sentenced the crew on the assumption
that the vessel was thoroughly seaworthy,
the law ordered a survey, with a view to
decide whether she was seaworthy or not, and
whether the captain shouldn’t have been
arrested instead of the crew, thereby showing
that it was by no means certain of the matter,
und had only gaoled the men on general prin-
Cl ples, as they were poor and friendless, and
no one was likely to make a row about them,
the surveyors announced that the lower part
0 , t " e J'igghig was somewhat worn and out of
culer. but that the upper part was in thoroughly
good condition. This was just the sort of
icport which might have been expected, as the
<ueiage marine-surveyor seldom ventures aloft,
uud he generally passes the upper part of the
Jggmg rather than risk his neck in strange
syniiiastic feats among the topmasts ; but even
118 statement tended to corroborate the crew’s
< ssertiong to some extent. Then it was dis-
yeied that the sentence of eight weeks’ im-

wj e ,4 P°Aver to award four weeks only,

An Old Tory Re-told.
] ei ,!:,l I’Per Mouse problem still drags its weary
of ?..i 4 ou B ’ u Maoriland as a solemn evidence
ikisr u VllA .y happen (o every country which
memW a 110 mmec Council. There’ are 38
pi'cscn/u 1U 1C -Maoriland Council at the
■ iimlcn, UC) ° f live arc cither absent,
Paralysed, or nthenviso. incapable!
leaving 33 available for service.. Of these 30
are old ponderous Conservatives representing
the Atkinson, Hall, Stout-Vogel and other
deceased Ministries and policies which the
country has long since abandoned, and exactly
three represent the Liberal party which is now
in power by the sanction of a vast majority of
the people. Seven of these Tory nominees were
appointed by Governor Onslow at the request
of the Atkinson Cabinet after that body
had been defeated, and had tendered its
resignation, but when it was holding
office provisionally pending the arrival of its
successor ; yet the same Onslow, who had filled
the Upper House with the derelicts of a dead
Conservative Cabinet, refused to make any
nominations to oblige the live Democratic
Ministry which succeeded it. His successor,
Glasgow, promises to be a little more amen-
able ; he is just Tory enough to be of no use, and
just Liberal enough to save himself from rotten
eggs and execrations. He offers to send up nine
Democrats to the Council, so instead of every
Radical measure submitted by the Ballance
Government being contemptuously thrown
out by thirty votes against three, it will
be thrown out by thirty votes against twelve,
and the results will be just the same, though
the figures will be slightly different. The
beauty of a nominee Chamber can never be
fully appreciated until a really Democratic
Cabinet is in office. If the Ballance Govern-
ment can hold on for 25 years, and as each
venerable Tory dies can fill his place with a
reliable Liberal, and can prevent its new
members becoming petrified and useless in the
interim, it may succeed in doing something at
last. But if it wants to do something now the
shortest way seems to be to stand over Governor
Glasgow with a shark’s-tooth club, and give
him the alternative—either lie must nominate
30 Democrats in one act or the club will come
down. It would be unconstitutional, no doubt,
but there is no way out of the difficulty that
isn’t unconstitutional.
The first sign of returning prosperity is visible
in Queensland —rents are beginning to go up.
The rise is noted principally in the sugar dis-
tricts, where property is reported to have
increased in value something like 20 per cent.
There is not the slightest sign up to date of any
rise in wages, nor is work any more plentiful,
nor are the unemployed any less hungry. The
people who were homeless before are still home-
less, and there is no improvement in their pros-
pects ; you can still get 200 applicants for a
miserable little clerkship at 15s. per week, and
all the roads leading west and north and south
are trodden hard by the feet of hundreds of
men looking for work and never finding it.
Under the regime of the big landlords the posi-
tion of the worker only begins to improve when
the revival of trade is well advanced, and
rents have been going up for a considerable
period. Sometimes the revival is only a
false alarm—it will probably prove so in the
present case—and then wages don’t rise at all;
after rents have gone up a little and property
has begun to sell at better prices the spurt is
exhausted. Moreover, the present movement is
confined to the sugar districts, and if it does
progress far enough to make an improvement in
wages it will only be an improvement in the
wages of the nigger-stealer, and the South Sea
pirate, and the Government agent, and the pub-
lican who fills these buccaneers up Avith rum
when they come back from a cruise among the
islands, and the ordinary Queensland labourer
isn’t in it. But, despite all these deductions, it
is a gladsome thing to hear of a sign of return-
ing prosperity anywhere or upon any terms,
and there is doubtless joy in heaven—or the
other place—when rents begin to go up. Most
likely it is in the other place.

In the case of M. H. Foran, avlio un-
successfully appealed to the Victorian Full
Court for a mandamus to compel the Minister
of Education to produce a letter which he had
received from Foran’s immediate superior
officer, and to which Eoran’s dismissal from
the Public Service was allegedly attributable,
the application was refused with costs against
the plaintiff. According to the English law
“the interests of justice, as far as individuals
are concerned, must be subordinate to the
interests of the whole State administration of
which the Department of Justice is merely an
integer,” and the Australian ruling is in accor-
dance with this statement. The sooner this
kind of philosophy is knocked on the head the
better. Foran is, in this particular, a victim
to a piece of tyrannical bureaucracy. That
without the welfare of the part the health of
the whole can exist, cannot be logically
admitted.
J. M. Davies, M.L.C., brother of Sir Matthew
of that ilk, became grieved and shocked when,
at a meeting of shareholders in the Real Estate
Bank, Melb., somebody referred to Agent-
General Munro as a “ disgrace,” whose appoint-
ment was a “ monstrous thing.” These observa-
tions pained Davies to such an extent that he
threatened to leave the room if they were
repeated. In effect, he was already protesting
against the possibility of dear M.vttheav being
reviled in public. Bluff is out of the question
now, however. Members of the old “repre-
sentative ” crowd can look lofty and shriek
“ Shame !” when the robber chiefs are spoken
of without reverence, but the poor devils
who have lost everything will be awed into
silence no more. The majesty that used
to hedge the financial gang of Melbourne
has gone for ever, like Munro and Matthew
I). Nobody respects anybody nowadays, and
unless the present Government have suffi-
cient pluck to make a holy show of several
people they themselves will be set down as
being no cleaner potatoes than the rest. Only
the other day Shiels was slobbering over the
late delectable Premier and his comrade in
greatness, Matthew Daa’ies. Each was a sad
loss to 11 1 c position he had' honoured, &c., &c.
Their names stank in the streets even then, and
now that the London Times calls one man a
rogue in the language of diplomacy, and the
other fellow is a “disgrace straight out, if
must he about time that Smuts, as the head of
his Gracious Queen’s Government, made a
public recantation of the faith that lit' re cully
professed.
Melbourne is agitating (in the columns of the
Age) for cheap fish. The better kinds of local
fish are unquestionably dear at present prices,
and the middleman takes the big profits, for
the destiny of dead fish, all the world over, is
to fatten the first buyer at the expense of the
last buyer, whilst the original catcher and the
small hawker do badly all the time. But the
idea that good fish will ever become cheap and
plentiful in Victoria is as absurd as the notion
that brown coal can be pushed into popularity,
A Victorian fisherman can do no more than
cast his net and haul it in, and when he hauls
it in he lias to look hard for anything that was
worth the netting. He can never expect to
net enough profit for himself to encourage a
rush of men into the same line of business, and
until hundreds more fishermen are engaged in
catching large quantities of tasteless fish for
the sake of the few palatable fish to be found
amongst them, the public will go on gorging
itself with meat as usual. The “sinful
waste ” of fish is a fact from which a fictitious
moral is drawn. A grey mullet destroyed
doesn’t necessarily mean a mullet wasted, except
to the indignant journalist who has never parted
a hard-earned shilling for mullet, and subse-
quently wept for his indiscretion. The Age
may dream dreams of “ delicious, wholesome
fish at a reasonable price,” but the reality is,
generally speaking, a mullet, an article no more
wholesome than a lump of yesterday’s bread
Avithout butter, and not half as delicious.

From an English paper :
It is pleasantly suggested by some party politicians
that if Thomas CoorEß, the once famous Chartist, had not
become a writer on Christian Evidence and a Liberal
Unionist, he would not have received a couple of hun-
dred pounds out of the Civil List. This is hardly the way
in which Mr. Musdella, who applied for the grant, looks
at the matter. He thinks that the First Lord of the
Treasury has done a very kindly thing in relieving the
necessities of an aged man whose literary talents are in-
disputable, and who during more than part of his life has
laboured hard to undo the evil he did in the other portion.
This sort of thing is often met with in the
servile English press. What is the atrocious
evil of which Thomas Cooper, “ the once
famous Chartist,” was guilty in early life?
Simply being in sympathy with a band of men,
in advance of their time in ideas, though
woefully behind it in their knowledge of the
best way to enforce their demands. Cooper has
lived to see the English people in possession of three
of the articles of the charter : Abolition of Pro-
perty Qualification, Ballot and (almost) Univer-
sal Suffrage (obtained in that order), and also to
know—if in the intervals of his researches into
Christian evidences he has time to take further
note of such sordid subjects—that nearly the
whole of what the English papers love to call
“ Greater Britain,” pays its representatives.
The only other thing advocated by the Chartists
was the weakest item, viz., annual Parliaments,
which nobody in the present day wants particu-
larly, so far as The Bulletin is aware. But
this is always the way. Because Cooper puled
on Christian evidence and avowed himself one
of that set of mongrels who are known as
Liberal Unionists he is held to have expiated
the sin of previously upholding the enlightened
principles, which his countrymen have since
affirmed, and has atoned for) his former evil
deeds.

To all appearances, gold will ere long become
as scarce as available land. Here are some of
the figures : Total value of gold in world’s cir-
culation, about £1,500,000,000, of which 670
millions are in the form of coin. World’s
annual output of fresh gold, 6,000,000 ounces,'
to which Australasia, the U.S., and Russia
contribute 1,745,570 ounces, 1,586,304 ounces,
and 1,120,695 ounces severally. Australasia
coins almost as much as she produces, i.e.,
£5,550,000, and nearly approaches the amount
put through by the London Mint, i.e.,
£7,500,000 each year. The world’s yearly
increase in gold is only one-third of a million
ounces, and of the entire production of six
millions of ounces, half is devoted to the pur-
poses of arts and manufactures. As the pror
ductiveness of the above-mentioned three
principal countries has been for the last four
years at an almost nominal rate of increase,
unless fresh fields shortly are discovered most
of the gold now in use for ornamental purposes
will have to go into the mints’ melting-pots, or
else the scarcity of coin in comparison to the
increase of general population will gradually
raise the purchasing power of the sovereign.
As Australasia is .just now producing just
enough to supply each head of her population
per annum with the magnificent sum of £2, and
as, during the last 20 years, four-fifths of her
total gold output has been exported to foreign
shores, it is as well to draw the attention of
local political economists to the future outlook,
and ask them to consider the advisability of
restraining the efflux of future “ yellow boys.”
It may be possible to improve State
organisation. It is quite impossible to improve
the conditions of trade as carried on by private
firms.
Dear Bulletin, —A “Jimmy Woodser”
may mean a solitary drink (or a solitary drinker)
in some places, a “ Jack Smithers” in others, but
in the Western district of Victoria, if a man takes
a drink by himself, he is said to “go Ballarat.” —

“ The orthodox theology is practically use-
less, or worse than useless, here. It first invents
a crime, and then invents a punishment, and then
invents a means of escaping punishment. Its
heaven is the reward—not for good and virtuous
conduct, but for believing in the ‘ plan of salva-
tion ! ’ Its hell is the reward—not for false and
evil deeds, but for heresy !” — Rev. O. Walters , in
“ The Criminal’s Ascension, or from the Gallows
to the Golden Streets.”
Dr. Manningtou Caffyn, of South Yarra,
Melbourne, has, by establishing a hydropathic
institution, supplied a great public want—long
keenly felt in Australia. He has recognised the
futility of the ordinary medicinal methods of
curing alcoholic and morphia-craving's and by
lifting the patient physically beyond the reach of
unwholesome appetites docs what drugs can never
do. The methods employed at Dr. Catfyn’s insti-
tution include massage, exercise, suitable food,
ami electric and steam baths. The results
achieved by these moans in skilful hands have been
marvellous.

The new catalogue of Melbourne Public
Library is nearly clone. It is in 120 drawers, full
of largish cards, each inscribed with all the par-
ticulars about a book, and where to find it. The
cards are upright, same size as the drawer. You
quickly run them over, all the drawers being in
alphabetical order. Thus names can be added or
withdrawn.

SIC Maoquarie-street North, Sydney, June 29, 1892.
Alv Dear Moorabinua,—
The Nov Imperial Opera House (why “Imperial” in
this happily Emperor-less land ?) which is the same old
Sydney Opera House with the addition of prickling palm-
leaves on the narrow, toilsome staircase, and artistic re-
upholstering of dragon-blue plush, with box draperies of
grey velvet brocade and dragon-blue silk—opened (Mr.
George Forbes is the manajah—which is—aw —awf’ly
jolly, donch’novv) last Saturday with a mild amount of
fashionable eclat-about as much as you meet at an
amateur show. But it all died out half-way through the
exasperating fiz-giir, or “ Whirligig,” or whatever it was,
and one killed time until one’s wheelbarrow was due at the
door by sitting in judgment on one’s neighbour.
Mrs. W. Mitchell’s get-up of ivory silk and silk guipure
with diamond necklace, was a thing of beauty and-a joy
to its wearer ; Mrs. Burdekin wore black, of course, with
a hunch of autumn leaves on the corsage ; Miss Burdekin
also-in black lace with pale terra-cotta ribbon hows about
it; Mrs. (Mayoress) Maiming kept on a cream opera-wrap
sprinkled o’er with pink roses and trimmed with white
fur; her daughter in a babyish white silk gown; pretty
Miss Bensusan wore black with a becoming knot of cox-
comb red in her hair. Lady Salomons was wrapped up in
a heckle-feather boa. There was also a bounteously-
f on tied lady on view who had gotten herself up most
gorgeously. Fancy any woman with village-blacksmith
arms widening them still further with a line of pink silk
down the upper side ! And just imagine her pronging
two big flaring roses in the side of her full-sized skull!
Behind the footlights the most unnatural thing was
when the male parvenu was made to enjoy himself amid
his gilded surroundings, while his wife, in red silk,
yearned for the hack-parlour over the shop. In real life
it’s always “the missus” who eggs her husband on to
C.M.G. lengths, isn't it ? Bella Bashall was the prettiest
personality on the stage, and looked very fresh in her
first frock,of lilac and dark-purple velvet, with flop hat
trimmed with myrtle and wheat; but in the second act
she should have worn what one’s grandmother called a
camisole with her white muslin gown—as it was, you got
to know too much about what she had on underneath.
By the way, Mrs. Bennett didn’t appear on the first
night, though it was announced in the newspapers, and
society was on the qui vice to see how she got on. As she
didn’t appear, society is still on the qvi vine. The ways
of theatrical managers are dark.
Miss Myra Kemble, resting in Sydney after a successful
M.L, run, seems to have enjoyed herself among the
Maoris. When the Co. disbanded, the members thereof
presented Myra with a pretty silver card-case. In a day of
empty treasuries and stranded companies, it’s pleasant to
hear of a tour going through on salary and ending as
above.
By the way, Miss Kemble’s “ Dr. Bill ” performance in
aid of the Sydney unemployed promises to be interesting.
It is to be played by Society amateurs, possibly in the
Centennial Hall, and the tickets will range to a guinea.
As Government House official mourning for Lord Ancram’s
death ended last Monday, it is possible that Captain
Leigh will fill the title-role, and Mr. Goschen take Cecil
Ward’s part, but nothing is definitely settled so far.
Let people who discuss the latest newspaper ’errors
with their olive-branches listening mouth-agape, take
warning from the recent nursery experiences of a Sydney
medico, who is not only a most skilful physician, but
looks like one. His two youngsters somehow heard a
great deal of Rainhill-raurder talk, and, being imitative
little rascals, resolved to “play Deeming.” As a result,
both are now in a critical state of health—the one from
being half-flattened-out with a stone supposed to repre-
sent the hearth, and the other from the whacking he got
from his pa.
Rather a novel show was afforded by fencing-master
Schuch’s pupils at Sydney Oddfellows’ Temple the other
afternoon, when parry, cut and thrust, with much stamp-
ing of feet and a sound in the air like the house-father
sharpening the carving-knife, filled in an hour or so very
pleasantly before dinner. Judging by the medical men
who straddled around the platform (for you get about
like a crab, or an aggravated case of bow-legs, when you
pursue your adversary with a skewer), fencing must be a
very hygienic pastime. Drs. Fiaschi, .Marano, Roth,
Bury and Ellis, Mr. Rolin, Mr. Windeyer, and Mr.
Gaulhiac, were the gentlemen who assaulted one another
with buttoned foils (in all bu.t three instances, when
snakey swords leaped forth, and you couldn’t see the
combatants for wire and wadding), and there were two
ladies—Mrs. Bowes and Miss Hawley—who pinked one
another in an amiable way early in the bill.
Fencing comes next to fiddling as an effective accom-
plishment for the woman with a good figure, but until
she leaves off the padded, awkward-looking cuirass and
hideous mask which are the safeguards when you are
sticking your man for exercise, she won’t have much but
her science to make her worth watching, believe me. By
the way, at the feeing Contest it was noticed that when
a lady was pitted against a trembling man she always had
last thrust. The ruling passion strong even in sword-
play, you see!
There was a crowd in the hall, headed by Lady
Windeyer, and including so many men that I couldn’t
help wondering how many happy invalids were having
one free afternoon, at least, to gel well in ? The audience
seemed to enjoy it, and the applause was gusty as the
wind outside—but what a pity they hadn’t music! A
pianissimo bar or two helps a thing like that immensely.
What would the “ Corsican Brothers’ ” duel be without
all that tremulous orchestration ?
Mr. M'Adoo’s Jubilee singers sound very tuneful in the
York street hall. For one thing, everybody is so articu-
late,, and in the idiotic nigger melodies it really is most
necessary to know what they’re all about. Mrs.
M‘Adoo’s tenor warblings are astonishing—she ought to
have worn a dress-suit instead of pale-grey silk and lace
to go with her voice. Miss Julia Wormlie’s recitations
are a success—yea, even to the extent that she only wants
a few judicious pot-plants, some fairy-lamps, and a
switch tailed frock to be Adelaide Deletion's hated rival.
What strange instinct is it that makes people send expen-
sive wreaths and things, with large visiting-cards pro-
minently attached, to the coffin of the aristocrat who
didn’t know ’em from Adam ? It’s taking an unfair
advantage, I consider, to pile expensive offerings on the
name-plate of a deceased individual, who would probably
have sent them back post-haste if they had been offered
to him living-but here again Jenkins, with ids vokn
miuous note-book, is at the root of it. The little line in
the “ noosepaper ” next day, “ Mr. and Mrs. Moses Isaac
Jacob’s names were attached to a lovely wreath with the
beautiful consolation, ‘There is no pershent in heaven,’ ”
is the reason why pushing people send very clearly-
printed cards hanging to pallid vegetables whenever Any-
body (with a huge A) shuffles off this mortal coil.
Lady Jersey attended the Earl of Ancram’s funeral
(with which the foregoing paragraph is absolutely uncon-
nected) and by doing so has cleared the way for lots of
women who want to hear the stones rattling on their clear
departed’s coffin, but have had to keep at home up to
date because it’s etiquette. Yet, in this particular in-
stance I think precept is better than even vice-rega 1
example. Women are mere bundles of surface feelings,
and there would be harrowing scenes in cemeteries it it
were socially proper for them to attend interments.
Besides, as they are the cause of most men’s deaths it is
only good taste for them to stay away from the burial of
their victims. Until they learn to conquer their sobs (at
the expense of a red nose, mind you '.) by gazing fixedly
at the lining of their hats in the superior masculine way,
women had better stand out of funeral processions, no
matter if Lady J. goes to a dozen in a week.
An influential committee is working up a grand com-
plimentary benefit concert to Mrs. Vandeveer Greene,
who leaves for New York on Bth Aug., next. The conceit
is in recognition of her “very valuable musical services
during her sojourn in N.5.W.,” and I hope the lady’s
many admirers will make it a success by attending in
astonishing numbers. It is not altogether decided— by
the way—that Mrs. Van. will study for the operatic stage
when she reaches Milan ; it is probable that she will
only further cultivate her lovely sympathetic voice for
the concert platform.
I heard an unusual sort of anecdote of a well-known
Southern professor of music. He was passing a house in
a Melbourne suburb lately in which a budding vocalist
was going through some flighty exercises on the wrong
vowels. Hearing which the professor rapped smartly at
the door and sent his card in with the polite inscription,
“ Professor Blank Dash’s compliments to the young lady
with the beautiful voice, and wishes to advise her to sing
her exercises to “la-la” instead of “do-re-ini.” It was a
very kind thing to do, of course—but in this eccentric
age of cheap advt. one is so prone to wink the oilier eye !
Monsieur Claudius Deslouis will shortly appear before
the world as a composer, lie lias recently finished a
song, “Les Saules” (The Willows), the words of which
are by’his son, Marcel Deslouis, whom the Parisian papers
term “a second Beranger.” “ Les Saules” is in C minor,
and ends most effectively on E natural.
It is interesting to know that Teddy Blueskin Royce
went to school with Herbert Standing. Some actors’
memories are too long for other actors’ looks. At the
Bernard Beere reception in Smellbourne, a lady sealed
next to the genial Teddy questioned him enthusiastically
about Mr. S.— did Teddy know him? and so on. “Madam,
we went to school together,” said Blueskin. “Indeed!
but wasn’t there some—er —discrepancy?” “ Madam, we
were both ten years old forty years ago !” said the candid
Teddy, while the Beere’s leading man looked unutterable
things across the table.
Teddy Royce’s benefit is to happen at the Criterion on
6th July, and everybody wishes him a bumper house for
the important occasion. Benefits, though, are often
expensive matters to the beneficiary, who doesn’t always
clear it by two figures, especially when lie or she has to
pay for the use of the theatre—but then you seldom hear
of that, because it’s more flattering to Thespian amour
propre to have the temple “kindly lent,” even if it’s only
on paper. One Australian firm of managers have a rule
never to lend their theatre to anybody-not even their
most invaluable mummer—except for a pretty round
figure, so it often happens that managers Sock and Buskin
get the bag instead of the beneficiare as onghter. What
appears to be, and what is, are hardly first cousins in
Stagehand, are they?
Apropos benefits, the reason alleged why Pattie Browne
didn’t get a Sydney send-off has more or less wounded
susceptibility in it from one point of view. And the other
person who might have roused to action had a scheme of
vengeance to wreak on somebody— so the green-room
gossip goes.
I am told that after a hank smash down South, the
other day, the authorities of a certain church were called
upon to reduce their overdraft, whereupon the incum-
bent, an old gentleman who draws in all about £l2OO a
year, recommended that the curate’s salary be reduced
from £3OO to £250. The curate has a wife and eight young
children, whereas his ecclesiastical superior’s family are
now all independent of their saintly pa. The wardens—
who were afraid to oppose the proposition—consented,
and, as the wretched curate couldn’t possibly manage on
so small a stipend with his house full of youngsters, he had
to make room for an old cheap-labour celibate clergyman.
Tiie curate is now trying to get work and his wife has had
to take a position as teacher.
The only difference to be detected in the prismatic
“La Cigale”—viewed again with a calm, eighth-night
expression—is that Charles Ryley’s beard has been worn
off, presumably by too close contact with Marie Gallon's
C sharp, which is sharp enough for any bartering pur-
poses. By the way, the over-proof limelight, brighter
than any that jets precipitated at George Henry V., is
rather unkind to the stage complexions of the company.
There was a sassiety circle on the eighth night. A
magpic-frocked theatre-party (it has been correct lately
to leave off parrot plumage on account of Lord A.’s death)
included the Misses Cox and Miss Clara Manning; but a
theatre-party in black and white isn’t a radiant chromatic
addition to the reserved seats. Carrie Swain wore a
pretty grey-and-pink frock with diamonds about it:
one lady in a big grey checked shawl drawn up « la
counterpane appeared to be snoring fitfully by her atti-
tude.

Another man who has carried arms in Her
Majesty’s service is charged with murder. J. J.
Cat ter, a private in the S. A. militia, shot his boon
companion through the heart last week, by way
of putting a moral finishing-touch to a review
whereat they had worked themselves into a blood-
thirsty frame of mind by blazing away with blank
cartridge. The Australian soldier is becoming an
important consideration in matters pert lining tJ
homicide.'

The greatest of all Mrs. Bernard Beere’s
successes in Sydney up to date is “Adrienne
Lecouvreur,” which was put on at the Criterion
on Saturday, and prospered exceedingly. It is a
profoundly pessimistic drama, and because of its
pessimism is all the more true to the eternal
verities. Its moral is that there is no poetic
justice either in heaven or eartli or—so far as
evidence goes—ln hades ; that all is not for the
best; that the fabled mills of God which are said
to grind the transgressor exceeding small are as
likely to grind up the saint as the sinner ; that
virtue isn’t its own reward ; and that blessed is he
who has the heaviest feet and the most callous
heart for lie shall inherit the earth. It is the story
of a great and noble woman who dies miserably,
while the gaudy, boastful soldier for whom she
sacrificed herself lives, probably to forget her and
be happy with someone else; and the rival who
killed her goes peacefully on her way ; and the
cynical priest who gibbered round all through the
five acts goes on gibbering indefinitely. Mrs.
Beere’s acting throughout this long tragedy
is a exhibition of true genius, and it is hard
indeed to find even one weak point in the char-
acter of the lost Adrienne. Next to her comes M.
Marius in whose hands the kindly, sympathetic
old Michonnet is not a dramatic character, but a
living reality. Herbert Standing as the callous
ecclesiastic with the absent soul lias a part that
just suits him, inasmuch as he is apt to be a
trifle hard and unsympathetic, and the Abbe is by
nature as hard as the nether millstone and the
upper one also. Maurice de Saxe, as played by
Otno Stuart, is a gaudy and sometimes rather un-
satisfactory soldier, who stands grimly upright
like a sentry during a great part of Adrienne’s
death scene, and lias bursts of violent emotion
when he isn't standing upright. The rest of the
show consists principally of Miss Isabel Urquhart,
who scores the greatest success she has yet
accomplished, and Mr. J. F. Catheart who does
likewise, and the Criterion isn’t large enough to
hold the public enthusiasm, and the drop-scene is
being fast worn to rags by much hauliug-up in
acknowledgement of the frantic applause of the
audience.
& * & *
The aged rafc and the bloated spider which
has no flics upon him, and the gopher which
walketh in darkness because his deeds are evil,
have been ejected from Sydney Opera House, and
the old establishment has resurrected in all the
glory of its fresh upholstery, and looks even newer
than many a new theatre can afford to look. The
circle is gay in its adornment of blue plush, and
the walls are an {esthetic green, and the roof is
green likewise, and the gaslights cast a greenish
radiance upon the audience, and the new curtain
is a dream of loveliness, and the now scenery is
altogether fair to look upon, and everything is
bright except the comedy of “The Whirligig”
which is sadder than a frozen gnome in a lifeless
world. The author —whose name isn't given—has
obtained three of the longest intervals that were
ever heard of, and round them he has wisped a
thin and very small comedy, which suggests
nothing so much as half a pound of jelly hung on
a vast skeleton—the skeleton being the great
desolation of waiting, and the other flabby sub-
stance the thing that happens when the waiting
is over. The plot is short and easily described.
In the first act a broken aristocrat’s estate is sold
to a rich grocer ; the aristocrat loves the grocer’s
daughter ; he and his sister and his maiden aunt
are left penniless; and the retired tradesman
generously gives them his old shop by way of a
fresh stare in life. In the second act the fallen
swell tries to run the grocery establishment, and
sells rough-on-rats in mistake for flour, and
otherwise fails to make the business pay; and in
the third act the grocery-man gets tired of his
unnatural splendour, and lie gives the haughty
scion the estate back again, also Ins daughter, and
his blessing. The shrieksorae humour of the
play consists in the circumstance that the
young man can’t find the tea, and that
the old one feels miserable at the sight of his own
flunkey, and when these two facts are exhausted
there is nothing more. The play is called “The
Whirligig,” because the grocer, in his last public
utterance, makes a pathetic allusion to the whirli-
gig of life, and his wife backs him up in his rash
statement. But in spite of these drawbacks, we
love the new comedy, and intend to go back and
see it persistently so long as it lasts ; we love it
for the calm restfulness of these three long, long
intervals—it even begins with an interval —for
their holy peace and Sabbath stillness—for the
dreamy sense of silence and sleep, and the absence
of all trouble. They are the best things of their
kind we ever met, and it isn’t every company that
knows how to put an interval on the stage so
that it will be a reality and not a mere painted
imitation.
# #
The lessee—our dear old friend, George
Forbes —has put a strong company into this
desperately weak comedy. The three principal
male characters are played by J. R. Greville,
Henry Shine, and George Lash Gordon, and it
would be hard to find a much stronger trio.
Also that lovely and diaphanous vision, Bella
Bash all, fills the part of the grocer’s daughter, and
Miss Rosa Conroy and Mrs. Edouin Bryer make
up a total of six individuals who would arouse
much enthusiasm if it were not that circumstances
are too heavy for them, and every separate ham
in the grocer’s shop uses like an accusing spirit to
state that enthusiasm, in snoh a show, would be a
wasted emotion. The only name in the whole
cast which creates even the slightest tendency
to execration is that of Miss Hardie, who has
a particularly bad part, and deserves one
that is many degrees worse. Also, there seems to
be every ebanee that she will get it sooner or
later. Even the first night audience was too much
crushed to applaud anybody ; it gave a cheer for
the new drop-scene, and then fell back frozen and
paralysed, and remained that way till the end,
except once, when Greville was heard murdering
cockroaches among the side-scenes, and then it
smiled a sad smile as jf it had been after cock-
roaches itself in otlieudays, and knew they were
hard to catch. YU
* *
“ La Cigale,” at Her Majesty’s (Sydney))
has not improved since it. was last heard of. It
never will improve, in fact; unless a new plot and
a new libretto are inserted, and everything is born
again right down to the gas-man, it must remain
as it is to the end. No one can make a chest of
drawers out of a sow’s ear, nor do figs grow in a
pawnshop, and these two Scriptural observations
express the situation exactly. Marie Halton, at
the time of writing, is announced to leave’for
Yurrup by the Polynesian, her place to be taken
by Miss Violet Yarley—who isn’t in any way
related to a certain awful butcher-evangelist of the
same name. “ Marjorie,” with Lauri and Henry
13racy in the cast, will be produced on Saturday
(J uly 2). At the Garrick “ The Bells ” are still j' u
evidence, and every night about 9.30 they tinkle
dismally among the snow-drifts, and a ghostly
sledge, with a ghostly horse in the shafts and a
ghostly Jew on board, stops at the door of Mathias
the burgomaster, and silently invites him to come
out dnd be lost. Also, a little comedy entitled
‘‘Up in the World” serves as a curtain-raiser. It
is built upon the central fact that the rich
Christopher Mudlark’s brother —in the green-
grocery line—calls on him and behaves like a
comic hog in the dining-room; and the moral is
that no matter how elevated we may be we should
never be ashamed of our humble brother Bill, but
should rather feel glad when he comes round in a
misfit white hat, and wants a winkle and a drop
of gin, and breathes an odour of fish upon our
daughter.

Sundry deserving shows, large and small,
are bearing up manfully against the irrevocable
edict that all theatres are grass. The sweet-voiced
Jubilee Singers are gradually tapering off at the
Centenary Hall. The human snakes and the
howling skeleton ai’e in good form at the School
of Arts ; and at the two aquariums (Bondi and
Oqogee) the damp, depressed fish, and the hippo-
grif, and the octopus, and the wild sardine raging
in his tin are as fresh as ever. A matinee benefit
is to be tendered to Mr. E. W. Royce, at the
Criterion, on Wednesday (July 0), and if the
results are equal to the occasion half Sydney will
be trodden under foot in the rush. At the Wax-
works a new clairvoyant and mystery man reveals
the past and the future, and tells you what your
wife did when you were out, and tells your wife
what you did when you were not in. A very
successful amateur performance was given on
Monday at the Garrick—kindly lent by Mr.
Bentley, under the supervision of Mr. Geo. Lash
Gordon—kindly lent by himself. The bill was
various, and the performance was a trifle better
than the average of its kind. Also, a reasonable
amount of business is doing on Saturdays and
Sundays at the Royal Standard.

Foli, who was only a qualified success,
lias given place to “ Bridget O’Brien, Esquire,” at
Adelaide Royal, and “The Shanghraun ” is to

Garner’s Rooms on 20th with “ La Mascotte,” and
Tommy Hudson is now farewelling at the Town
Hall. Mrs. Bornard-Beere opens on Tuesday,
J uly 13, and leaves for England two weeks later.

A strong force of the B. and B. company
appeared at Melbourne Bijou on Saturday in
comedy ancient and modern, an audience worthy
of the occasion having braved the stormy wind
that blew “round the town” meanwhile. Tither-
adge, Sass, Miss Temple and Mrs. Brough started
the double bill with a polished performance of “In
Honor Bound,” a fin dc siecle story often told in
the firelight upon the'same boards but never quite
so effectively told before. The purified version of
Wycherley’s “ Country Wife,” played in Sydney
a fortnight back, forms at least a bright, interest-
ing contrast to the polite gloom of Grundy’s
comedy. Pattie Browne in boy’s clothes would
appeal to the popular taste at any time, and now
that the days of Miss B. are numbered, and the
date of her farewell benefit is announced, an
opportunity of seeing this versatile vision at her
best ought not to be missed. The humour of
“The Country Girl” is too thin and bitter to
delight a depressed community, nevertheless.
Any sympathy that the old play arouses is claimed
by the man who gets hopelessly left at the finish,
and whose chief fault lies in the fact that he has
just turned fifty.
* * *
Moody’s desire to marry his tasty little
ward seems a pardonable weakness, and if the
fooling of him under aggravated circumstances is
a satisfactory purpose for a play then “The
School for Scandal ” should have held Sir Peter
Teazle up to scorn. Had Moody married his
rustic charmer before bringing her to town, as
Teazle did, she would evidently have thrown him
over without a shade of compunction for the first
Belleville who came along. But no matter. Pattie
Browne contrives to make a fascinating picture of
a pitiless young angel, whilst her speaking of the
epilogue softens down the hardness of its senti-
ment. Lawrence repeats his Sydney success in
the part of Moody, and the company is as before
witli two exceptions. Cecil Ward does all that
can be expected of Harcourt, and Miss Brenda
Gibson, much disguised, wrestles with the un-
suitable character of Alithea. To-night (Thurs-
day) the Drapers’Association wifi form a “push-
ing young party ” in the upper-circle, which por-
tion of the house was to be reserved for junior
footballers last night. On the afternoon of Friday
next (July Bth) sweet Pattie takes her farewell
ben. under vice-regal patronage.

At the Princess’s there were not the
crowded attendances looked for during last week.
The Foli concerts, supplemented in each instance
by an act of Italian opera, didn’t fetch the
mythical musical world to any great extent, so
the musical world may consider itself an exploded
delusion entirely. The bulk of its population
don’t exist for paying purposes, and take no
interest in sweet sounds which can’t be appre-
ciated on the nevah. A truly artistic season will
wind up to-night (Thursday) with a last perform-
ance of “ Lucia,” and when the curtain falls upon
Signora Cuttica & Co., in this, their finest effort,
the Melbourne public can safely assume that they
will never look upon such a show again at the
same reasonable price.

George Rignold is due at the Princess' on
Saturday. He will give them his big production
of “A Midsummer Night’s Dream,” which hasi»
pleasant sound these cold times, anyway, "he
Bard’s fantastic poem comes as a complete stranger
to the young people of Melbourne, who have
mostly been born since the “Dream” was last
interpreted within the radius of the Yarra’s smell,
and for this and several other reasons the revival
is likely to obtain the success it deserves.

George Musgrove announces that the lire
at Sydney Royal has reduced his original arrange-
ments to ashes and forced him to substitute a new
lot. “ Joan of Arc ” must quit the Opera House
in another week to make way for a second season
0 f Mrs. Bernard Beere. The Gaiety crowd will
then depart for Adelaide (a holy city where they
are tolerably certain to make a profound impres-
sion), and maybe they will afterwards resume the
broken season at the Opera House before attack-
j n g Sydney. Meantime, “Joan of Arc” is
flourishing. Opinions differ as to whether this
burlesque is better than ‘Faust Up to Date,” but
probably it is equally good. How good the other
may have been is also a mixed question.

The Alexandra, it appears, is not to remain
n sole possession of the rats that were addicted
to ravaging round the dress-circle even before the
shutters went up. On Saturday, these capacious
premises will be re-opened under the management
of Alfred Bootliman and Joseph Stark, who
intend to produce hair-lifting dramas at “prices
to suit the times.”

The Allred Dampier benefit at the
Princess's resulted in a sum of about .£250 for
that estimable actor and citizen, who, in the
words of Harwood, stood without reproach in the
centre of the stage and returned thanks in a feel-
ing speech. This kindly oration divulged the fact
/hat Mr. Dampier still cherishes the hope of
tightening the bonds tiiat bind us, &c., by taking
a native-born company to London and awakening
the people of that village to the beauties of the
Australian romantic drama. But we thought
George Darrell was about to give ’em fits in the
nine direction.

Other Melbourne shows are pegging away,
die Cogliills do well enough at St. George’s Hall,
whilst Dan Tracey and Billin hold out at their
respective fortresses. The Tattooed Man at the
Waxworks is filling a long-felt want, and the odd
fact that the cruel Indians forgot to tattoo his
face doesn’t shake the gentleman’s own faith in
his interesting yarn whicli he has been telling for
10 long, weary years and ought to believe
thoroughly by this time. On Thursday and
Saturday there will be Fob Farewell Concerts at
Town Hall.
At Poverty Point.
Well, you could fancy Dampier receiving the testi-
monial—'not exactly the crushed tragedian—not exactly.
Talk about Napoleon surrounded hy his generals ! The
gorgeous modesty of dear old Alfred, the faint disclaimer,
the raised eyebrows, the orbs wandering to the chandelier
direction, the sigh, the twist of Ids left leg.
Mrs. Bernard Beere may appear at a different theatre
on her return to Melbourne. A London paper commis-
erates her on the successor of her deceased husband,
Deering, having just come into a baronetcy.
“Crainond Brig ” hasn’t been played on the Australian
metropolitan stage since Lilians was here. Lilians, once
stage-carpenter at Edinburgh Royal, was coached in his
art by author Murray himself and afterwards hy Wynd-
ham. Boisterous, hut not a bad Scotch comedian, Lilians
shone as Bailie Nicol Jarvie in “Rob Roy,” Dandie
Dinmont in “Guy Mannering ” and Jock Hovvieson in
“Cramond Brig.” In the latter piece, a great favourite
in the hungry days of the stage from its introduction of
haggis and sheep’s head—Nellie Stewart, fresh from the
hands of Miranda—whither she was daily escorted hy a
young woolbroker who waited for her in Carlton Gardens
—sang her first solo, “Jessie, the flower of Dumhlane,”
on the stage of Melbourne Royal. Soon after the Stewart
family went busking in “ Rainbow Revels.” By the way,
Lilians, great pals with Solicitor Lavey and Jimmy Alison,
both since dead, being over-fond of brandy and hell-
toppsrs, got sunstruck, at Benalla, and returned to Scot-
land only to die.
“Tommy ” Hudson, who has made a tidy fortune in
minstrelsy, has given up travelling, and has secured a
seven years’ lease of Garner’s Rooms, Adelaide. Tommy
owns much property in and about Adelaide.
Another idol-broker, a diamond-eyed kincoh one. , The
Potter and the Bellew have “ amicably dissolved partner-
ship.” Hard-hearted, cold, business-like event! Bet you
Bellew cut the painter, though he was burnt out of all his
wardrobe, and Mrs. L. saved hers. Will Menelaus take
Helen back ?
There is something very pathetic about the esteemed
Dampier’s evidence in Miss Lily’s divorce-case: “The
respondent apologised for being drunk, but I informed
him that it had happened too often, and I must dismiss
him. The next I heard of him was that he was my son-in-
law.” Always the way.

To be husband of a great actress is a lucrative billet,
nowadays, but the divorce-courts are spoiling it.
“Mr. Jersey,” the latest English turf recruit who has
registered colours, is said to be no less a personage than
Mrs. Langtry. This looks as if the Lily had not quite
“chucked” Squire Abington Baird.
Fanny Rohina has gone hack to the music-halls. Also,
Kate Santley has returned to the line from whence she
came when the century was about 25 years younger.
Poor old Kate Santley ! Not that Kate is so antique
personally, but her celebrity is on its last legs.
Folly for Foil to keep to three or four songs.
How the Gaiety ladies all hobble ! Do they wear tight
boots ?
Grease the fat—Widow O’Brien is to have a Melbourne
benefit.
Spagnoletti: “ Met Dick Ireland’s son in Melbourne to-
day—the old man lives in him.
Odd that Foil won’t sing in opera when he sings with an
opera Co. Ain’t the poor little Cutticas good enough?
Bessie Bellwood quarrelled with her Manchester, hut
the London Dwarf has had to apologise for saying so.
When they do quarrel, they do. He tugs her hy the hair
out of the first-floor windy, and the hair comes off. We
apologise.
One of the Sells Bros., interviewed, in America, about
their circus in Australia, said “an officious reporter”
gave them away about the horses. Yet they didn’t think
ihey were thus out of pocket. What surprised them was
the expensive seats being well patronised, on the average,
while the cheaper seats were not so good—a melancholy
evidence, says the circus-man, of labour-depression in
Australia.
The Music Hall, a little horgan of the song and-dance
purfesh, is very angry with Irving and Johnny Toole for
talking slightingly of the “Boom-de-ay” school of art,
or something. And the Music Hall has said in its haste
a few unkind things about poor old Johnny, which are
worth the notice of certain Anglomaniacs who found fault
with The Bulletin, two years ago, for rating the “cele-
brated English comedian” at his true value. “Not
having seen every had actor,” snarls the candid publica-
tion in question, “ it might perhaps he unfair to say that
Mr. Toole is the worst of all possible actors, hut he is so
near an approach to the worst that the difference is
scarcely perceptible. For years he has strutted, an
uncouth, gibbering, lamentable ohjec , on a stage that has
long ceased to want him, and successive failures have
plainly indicated the public desire that (his ghost of
antique comedy should he ‘laid.’” There is about as
much solid truth in the above as in (he greasy press
notices and puff paragraphs on which J.L.T.’s reputation
contrives to exist.
Courtneidge, of the Gaiety Co., is a blend of two
familiar people. The biz. is the biz. of Snazelle, but the
voice is the voice of Teddy Royce.
One of the ladies iu “Joan of Arc” wears, well—and
deuced little else. The contour of the tout ensemble so
horrified the good old Argus that the damsel wore an
extra diamond ring next night. Nevertheless, it was two
Argus men, suspected of belonging to the Y.M.C.A., who
wrote a couple of Ta-ra-ra verses for Lonnen, containing
the line—“ Lord, you should just see me booze ! ”

she won’t meet WAV. in some out-of-the-way part of the
world, just when she has forgotten all about him? That
kind of beastly awkward tiling is always happening.
They would like to open the Sydney Gaiety season with
“Joan of Arc,” but the props have to he sent ahead.
Raynham and the rest altogether miscalculated the

played out, “ Forced After Date.” As for the “ Carmen ”
—probably it won’t see the lightatall, for its honnebouche
was the Bogie Man.

The local drapers have engaged the Melbourne Bijou
for a night. One of the most frightful rows ever known
at Drury Lane, London, was when Uowton, for his benefit,
put up a piece called the “Tailors of Tooley Street.” The
gallery was chockful of tailors, who stopped the play, the
signal being throwing a pair of scissors on the stage.
Madame Melba is reported to he about to re-visit
Australia to play for 12 nights in Melbourne and Sydney,
under engagement to Musgrove. The alleged season is
to begin in Melbourne on September 24, with Williamson’s
Opera Company. After that the lady starts for New
York, where Abbey has offered her the earth in return
for a spring season of 50 nights.
The leader-writer of the Age calls “ our
fighters” (Slavin and Peter Jackson) Anglo-
Saxons. Wonder what he would call Syme ?
Sixteen men have just driven 2500 cattle
from the Gulf of Carpentaria to the Hunter River
(N.S.W.), 2300 miles, and are only 25 head short.
The burglarising of a policeman’s house in
a Melbourne suburb, a few days ago (they stole all
the jewellery—except the bracelets), brings to
one's memory the time when the Melbourne Gaol
Governor was robbed of his fowls at that estab-
lishment.
Some years ago a large Sheep-station
was being sold in Melbourne, and among
those present at the auction was a rich Sydney
man, who, evidently, had been looking on the
wine when it was red. Bidding started, and soon
the Sydney man joined in, and kept on nodding
to the auctioneer until the station—which he had
never seen in his life and knew of only by name —
was knocked down to him for a few hundreds of
thousands. This little transaction got the Sydney
man into difficulties, from which he has never
been able to extricate himself.
It was a pretty cold-blooded and brutal
thing for a couple of hundred prominent citizens
to meet in Sydney, on Monday night, at a big and
expensive guzzle in honour of Dr. Ren wick, Com-
missioner to Chicago Exhibition, while so many
hundreds of poor people were starving all around.
These heavy, hog-like feeds are got up, not so
much to glorify the swollen-headed mediocrities
whom they are'osteniiiblv designed tohonour, as to
afford a set of smug and flatulent social greasers
opportunity of stoking their monstrous stomachs
and airing their still more monstrous vanity. Just
look down the guest-list.
ON THE ROAD.
A Plea for the Country.
When the city’s sons of Thespis from the “ army ” or the
ballet
Storm the suffering country people and unroll their
“ pops ” in view,
And begin with bowl and dagger with three direful acts
to dally,
Then we wonder why in thunder don’t they give us
something new.
Since we saw their plays in Sydney, time has grown senile
and hoary,
Still we strive to waken pleasure, hut we find our strife
in vain,
For departed is our relish for all prehistoric story—
We have heard them in our childhood, must we hear
them yet again ?
Is it but to kill the longing for the good old melodrama?
Is it hut to point a moral that our feelings are out-
raged ?
Proving thus the/drama’s horrors to the simple-minded
farmer,
Is this why these seedy supers all were specially
engaged ?
How we long for something recent, how we sigh for “Art
and Ruskin,”
In the place of hlood-and-thunder dramas of the long
ago,
Can we wonder at the terror folks have for the sock and
buskin
When the mournful country drama reeks of agony and
woe?
West Maitland.
W.A.S.
The Wild-Cat Column.
With what grim satisfaction must the aren-
liend of the Sydney bogus-bank industry, who has got
clean away, he watching the ruin he has made. He, who,
starting without a shilling, has handled hundreds of
thousands, lived on the fat of the land in three continents,
and sent his comparatively guiltless enemies to gaol as
felons.
# * #
An unfortunate correspondent asks us what
chance he has of recovering £250 lost to him in a fire by
the Trans-Pacific Insurance Co.? No more chance than
he would have of now getting a dividend out of the estate
of the late Emperor Nero.
# * #
British depositors in the Mercantile Bank have
decided in favour of compulsory liquidation, whereas Mel-
bourne depositors, being constitutionally incapable of
asserting themselves, left the shareholders to arrange for
a peaceful longwinded winding up hy Graham Berry and
two others. Unfortunately, the liquidator appointed hy
the Court in England will have no authority to raise Cain
on this side. Not that anybody wants to raise Sheol if
Graham Berry can satisfactorily prove that James Munro
didn’t sell a lot of paper to the local Mercantile Bank
shortly before it closed. Or show that if Jimmy did dis-
count £23,000 worth of hills as alleged, the said docu-
ments were worth the depositors’ cash paid for them.
For_ clearing up mysteries of this kind an aggressive
official liquidator is worth 50 friendly winders-up, some-
how. Every depositor in the Mercantile is directly in-
terested in knowing whether Munro negotiated those hills
of rumour, and, if so, why a moribund institution came
to change good gold for paper in its last moments, and a
free and independent depositor i.s entitled to howl for the
facts of the case.

Can it possibly bo true that a retired N.S.W.
official assignee has something near £50,000 of unclaimed
dividends in his paws which lie refuses to hand over to
Ihe Government, on the plea that lie alone is answerable
to the claimants (999-1000lhs of whom will assuredly
never turn up) if they ever do demand the cash ? A short
Bill indemnifying this gentleman would soon settle the
question. An almost similar state of things obtains in
Victoria.
• * •
One of the humours of the deceased Australian
Banking Co., of Sydney—that demoralised little institu-
tion whicli used to lend monish on furniture and bedding
atshixty per shent—was an attempt it once made to
induce the N.S.W. Government to open an account with
it. There was a large sum in the Treasury at the time,
and the hank got inflamed at the thought of so much
coin lying around loose, hut Treasurer M'Millan didn’t
respond to the overtures worth a cent. What a high old
time these hankers would have had if £250,GC0 or so of
Government money had been deposited over their shabby
little counter—what a rush for overdrafts—and what a
general granting of loans all round ! The spectacle of a
monkey eating hank notes would have been nothing to it.

# # *
Unique mining manager's report from Croydon,
N.Q.: —“ Iron Duke and Nancy Dee G.M. Co., Ltd.—Mr.
George Neal, manager, reports: During the fortnight 1
have sunk four shifts, then started to clear away the
mullock on the surface and erect a whip pole. I also
built a store-room, which was very much required, as we
had no place in which to put away the tools. Amongst
other preliminary work 1 erected a yard for the whip
horse. We are now awaiting a trial of a horse under
offer. I have ordered a new half-inch wire rope, but shall
have to wait some time for it as there are none obtainable
in town. The old windlass rope is now too short for the
work. I have also got a trolly to run the mullock away
from the shaft. Everything is in good working order and
ready for straightforward work.
