# * #
The Sydney banks have a plethora of money,
and the rate of interest stands at 4J per cent. Yet at the
present market price Federals and Commercials of Aus-
tralia yield nearly 10 percent., Cities 8, and Joint Stocks
6j. To those whose interest means perhaps their only
source of income, and who are not keen enough to watch
the market, the safest method is, of course, fixed deposit.
It is rarely that the depositor does fiot get a full return in
case of collapse. We are referring, of course, to hanks
proper. The defunct New Oriental should, it is estimated,
pay a two-thirds dividend, hut then there is no uncalled
capital to fall back upon.

An outspoken correspondent wants to know how
about the 2000 tons of reserved 2oz. ore, alleged to he
accumulated on Faulkner’s mine at Lamhula, and all
about the Pam bill a G.M. Co. with “its stores of 15oz.
stone,” and why Ml. Gahan can pay a monthly sixpence
dividend, while these mines, of which a great deal more
has been said, pay none? Well, here you are:—There is
no heap of 2000 tons accumulated at Falkner’s. There are
several hundred tons, but the present writer would not
like to wager that it would yield more than 2oz. by ordi-
nary treatment, or that as much is in it. The Lamhula
mine has no 15oz. store of ore, hut a heap of about 250
tons, whicli might yield an ounce per ton. The difference
between Mt. Gahan and these two claims is apparent to
anyone who has seen them. The former has a fairly big
body of rock, with the gold well distributed through it,
while the other two have small shoots, which are or have
been rich here and there.
#* ' *
There- is at least one good mine near Drake,
N.S.W., owned by Rivers and party, who, without outside
assistance, have opened up I heir mine and had regular
profitable emailings for nearly two years. It is a peculiar
formation which averages 2oz. per ion. The battery is a
miserable little thing. Pity the yields are not published
to encourage the industry. The publication of all yields
from private claims is compulsory in 311'.
The Rise and Shine mine is in N.S.W. on the
Q. border at Toolooin, where many thousands of ozs. were
won from the early alluvial. It was found hy Payne and
son, who extracted 1500ozs. hy dollying in a quicksilver
bottle. It is a small ironstone lode and is now all well
opened by a Brisbane Co. The gold occurs in very riel)
pockets, a characteristic of all ironstone mines.
# # #
The Bulletin anathematises the N.S.W.
prospecting vote as a useless fraud on the public
revenue. The writer knows that in some instances 30s.
a foot has been received and tne work done for £1- the
receivers pocketing the balance ; in others they manage
to get “measured up ” hy the warden’s clerk just ere the
shafts fall in. Subsidies from the vote are recommended
by officials who know nothing of mining, and tens of
thousands are being thrown away hy a hard-up Govern-
ment. Where is there to-day a paying mine the result of
the N.S.W. prospecting vote? or, where is there a field
anywhere, with a reasonable show, that lacks prospecting?
Again we say, let the money he spent in establishing a
mining college, with big metallurgical works to treat
difficult ore and teach Australian youth. Hundreds of
parents would pay big premiums for such a training for
their sons.
# # #
Australia is badly mixed in its silver-mining
industry. Silver was first profitably worked at Boorook
(N.S.W.) near the Q. border, hy one Horton, who worked
it for gold, and when he cleared up got silver instead of
gold amalgam. The metal was in the form of an anti-
monial sulphide found in quartzite. With the assis-
tance of an English expert, Mr. Davy, the mine
was worked for years with splendid results. Boorook
subsequently fell into the hands of some Mount Mor-
ganaires who abandoned it upon the richness of the latter
mine being revealed.
* * #
Something which looks permanent has been
found near Dalmorton, N.S.W. It is known as “ Cadeli’s
Claim.” Several parallel veins running in a black slate
are carrying gold to the extent of 3oz. per ton, and are
opening up well.
# # #
White Rock (capital =£1,(303,000), the gigantic
silver mining failure which was fl< ated on lies and cham-
pagne, has receded into that primeval loneliness from
which many a ruined man wishes to Heaven it laid never
emerged. There it stands, with the paint hard y mhhed off
its gigantic plant, a monument 10 human madm ss in
times of booms and booms!ers. It lias tlie magnificent
engine which supplied the electric power of the whole
of Melbourne Internalioual Exhibition; a sixty-head
plant built iu Chicago ; sevi ral massive boilers, a number
of true vanners, steam pumps, numerous dams, a tram-
way, and a hundred other appliances all costly and new.
All erected for what?-a lot of irregularly-defined small
galena veins which cannot he min d without taking away
the body, wnich will only average some seven or eight
ounces of silver to the ton. Yet it is only the king of
many similar failures which have squelched an industry
that might now he supporting an additional 10,000 ,mcii.
Mining men can as well ;.s others excuse a mining mis-
take, hut White Rock was'a palpable failure to any mining
man worthy of the name from the jump. A big American
mysteriously reported well of it. Subsequently another
American visited it, and expressed his surprise as follows:
“ Great Heavens ! Did he speak well of this? If so, I
guess he must have carried the ore away with him,’ 1

The newspapers are helping the manager of the
Mount Allen mine, away in the Ear West, to revive the
boom hy sensational reports which take one’s breath
away. Read slow: “Blanket tahl.s now ready to he
placed in their respective pos’tions ; a new piece of belt-
ing has been stretched on the old piece and jointed, and
is now in its position on the wheels.” We can see the
boom coming.
# * # ,
Never in Australian mining history were there
so many mining plants in course of sacrifice as at pre-
sent. It is the natural aftermath of the boom. There
certainly was a time when the erection of Costly
machinery was accepted as prima facie evidence of value.
A syndicate for the purchase of second-hand mining
plants should pay.

One of the pleasures of travelling to Manly
by the fast and commodious steamship Emu, nlias
“Brightside,” or one of the other boats, is.that
while sitting in the alleged smoking-room yon
occasionally have a big black coffin, obviously con-
taining a dead body, dumped down right in front
of you by a couple of red-nosed undertaker's men.
Little sights like these always please people..
Dear Bulletin, —Your yarn about the
confusion between a comp, and a litterateur in the
same office and bearing the same name, reminds
me of something that lately occurred in an Aus-
tralian metropolis. (I shall now invent fictitious
names, but every other particular is true.) There
were two brothers, so much alike that everyone at
a glance would swear they were two brothers.
One was a literary man whose name was Mop. The
other was canvasser for a paper “with the largest
circulation,” &c., named Besom. Whence the
difference in name ? Well, I don’t exactly know.
All I do know is that the family name is a
hyphened abomination—Mop-Besom. Mop, for
reasons satisfactory to himself, had dropped the
Besom end of his appellation. He had just failed
gloriously with a country paper, and was in the
metropolis dodging process-servers on account of
unpaid libel-costs. Besom was in full fling, a
sober, respected and solvent member of society.
A bailiff, in full hue and cry after Mop, has that
individual pointed out to him. He starts in mu-
suit and taps’Mop on the shoulder. He explains
his errand. Mop peruses the document. “ Ob,
thuds all right,” he says, with such an air of
childlike stupidity that ’tvvere irreligious to dis-
believe, “but you’ve got the wrong man, that’s
all. You want my brother, who goes under the
name of Besom, and is a canvasser for the Daily
Bangletop.” Bailiff apologises, and, in sheer
contrition, shouts. Mop “sherries.” Bailiff
rounds up Besom, gets frightened out of his seven
senses after doing a preliminary bounce, and then
rushes back for Mop’s hotel. Alas ! the man had
gone to Timbuctoo, and left an unpaid board-bill
behind him. —Yours, The Dipsomaniac.

SYDNEY
Personal Items.
Tlie Earl of Glasgow appears to be re-
garded by MaorUanders as “a jolly old buffer,”
Gladstone, it is cabled, has been struck in
the eye with a bread-crust, thrown by a woman.
. It looks as though Lord Ancram had died
that his name might be spelt correctly in the
papers.
“Chevalier of the Legion of Honor” is
amid G. C. Levey’s titles, on the title page of his
new book.
The Griffilwraiths call their dictator “the
champion draughtsman of Australia.” Black-
draughtsman !
Premier Holder (S. A.) was a State peda-
gogue, then newspaper-proprietor and editor, but
descended to politics.
Lord Ancram’s father—the Marquis of
Lothian —was offered the N.S.W. Governorship
when Oarington resigned.
Queen Victoria is suffering from severe
depression and melancholy. She hasn’t scon
Burdett Smith for ever so long.
Cornelius Proud and George H. Cargeeg,
two of Adelaide’s most successful brokers, were
once pressmen. Both are very pious.
London Times has struck what will pro-
bably prove the key-note for a coming chorus of
condemnation by calling Matthew Davies an
“unscrupulous financier.”
Luek is supposed to attend the ventures
of seventh sons of seventh sons, and yet a man
named Septimus de Septimo Somcthing-or-other
went bankrupt in Sydney the other day.
Milner Stephen, the “ faith-healer,” skips
about Melbourne —no older these 20 years. Such
ineffable conceit keeps a man under a glass-case,
where not a speck of mortuary dust can reach.
Gilbert Casey, the organising genius of the
A.L.F. (Q-), is a little blue-eyed chap with the
energy and virility of a green-head scrub ant. He
was a tram-cleaneronce,butthecompany “sacked ”
him.
Speaker Norton is credited with Brisbane
Courier’s political leaders. They reek with anti-
labour venom. Whenever Hodgkinson touches a
pen for print he gives himself right away, high-
falutin !
The reason why Melbourne society so
deeply resents Lady Hopetoun’s haughty frigidity,
is that it is well aware that, up to a little while
ago, the name of her family was not De Moleyns,
but plain, ugly Mullins, bedad.
There is a strong religious element in the
Holder (S.A.) Ministry. Holder is a local-
preacher, Cockburn and Gordon are warm Salva-
tionists, and Gillen is a staunch Catholic. Squatter
Handyside represents the unregenerated.
Blacker, the newest member of S.A,
Assembly states it as his ambition to see that
“all the on-educated children of the colony is
teached.” He also desires the appointment of “ a
commission to investigate into the farmers.”
Ex-Premier Playford (S.A.) says be is
“ done with political leadership,” and after this
session contemplates retiring to his market-garden,
where he will end his days smoking his favourite
clay-pipe and contemplating the tomato-crop.
S.A. Premier Holder and Lauds Minister
Gillen are South Australian natives; Chief Sec-
retary Cockburn, Education Minister Gordon,
and Works Minister Handyside, are Scotchmen ;
Attorney-General Stock came from England as an
infant.
Three “ Waterloo veterans” celebrated the
anniversary of that legendary struggle in Bris-
bane. Their ages totalled 270 years, and not one
of them could be convinced that the other two
were not fossilised impostors. Probably they were
all right.
The great ambition of Chief Justice Way
(S.A.) is to be made a Privy Councillor. He has
declined knighthood twice already as he considers
that to be too common a distinction. He is a
bachelor, and so has no one to consider but himself
in his refusal to be cheaply decorated.
John M‘Ewin, parson and journalist, of
Adelaide, is very anxious to get into Parliament-
He runs a non-union printing-office, is a bigoted
teetotaller and a rabid anti-smoker. He once
wrote: “How can the Holy Ghost dwell in a
breast saturated with tobacco smoke?”
The latest is that Deeming once tried to
mash Queen Victoria with a view to becoming
Prince Consort No. 2. Also, that he failed by but
a hair’s-breadth. Likewise, that it was Wales’
fear that F.B.D. would ultimately develops into
King Frederick I. that caused the rejection of the
petition to the Privy Council.
Old miser Costly, who bequeathed £130,000
to found the charities at Auckland (M.L.) that
bear his name, had intended to leave his entire
fortune to a long, red-headed youth, the son of a
casual acquaintance. But finding that the lad
was keeping a racehorse, and discounting the
future generally, he changed his mind on his
deathbed.

It was the just-deceased Tom Pavey, Mel-
bourne attorney, who defended O’Farrell, over
the Clontarf business, and instructed Aspinall. It
will be remembered how stupid the Sydney prac-
titioners were, in their toadying, which went to

despised them, and ostentatiously walked arm-in-

lickspittles honorary-membership of a leading-
club.
The report of sharebroker Derbin Willtlcr’s
death—he disappeared, while in very ill-health,
from a Queensland steamer —was quite the Collins-
street topic. He and broker Butters once raised
A 10,000, in a very few day, for Melbourne
Hospital. Formerly he was a queer, religious
crank, and got baptized in the sea, at Geelong, by
Missionary Westwood. Wilder was an estimable
man, with a not inhuman weakness for keeping
himself in evidence.
Old Jock Nimmaw lias “ stairted ” on a
pilgrimage to the birthplace of Bobbie Burns. He
loft Melbourne by mail steamer last week, un-
wept, unhonoured, and unsung. Nobody gave
him a farewell banquet, nor did the members of
the late Victorian Ministry roll up to bid their
former colleague adieu and wave their umbrellas
until the vessel was almost out of sight. Jock
Nimmo, alas ! is hopelessly played out. He’s a
decent old fellow who has “filled many public
offices” which lie never ought to have filled, and
made an object of himself in Parliament for 19
years, but now Jock is as publicly and politically
dead as the dodo. The brither Scots who hold
him up have dropped him, and seeing that there
never was a sufficient reason for supporting woolly-
headed J.N. in the first instance, he isn’t such a
real object of sympathy as would appear on the
surface.

stealing ;
At Roebounie Police-court a native named Cutchin, for
stealing a quantity of provisions from Lockyor Brothers’
station, at Tableland, was sentenced to a month’s im-
prisonment, with 12 strokes on leaving the gaol.
This is the sort of law Property will make when-
ever it gets the power.

Andy Lynch, the one-time member for
Carcoar in N.S.W. Assembly, could never be
brought to understand the significance of a
“pair,” On a critical division in ’7B he induced
another member of the same party to leave the
House with him, under the impression that thus
they gave their side two votes and somehow robbed
their opponents of two !
We saw something the other night that
was either high-toned courtesy or amazing cheek,
only we can’t decide which. There was a tall,
weary-looking man in a fruit-shop, and the girl
was getting some oranges out of the window for
him. She was a very handsome girl. The
window-ledge was broad, and it sloped down-
wards, and the oranges were at the very front,
right up against the glass. The girl reached for
them, and the next we knew she lost her balance,
and, with a wild scream, her feet went up and she
was falling right into the plate-glass. In another
instant th -re would have been a fearful smash,
and the gi 1 and the ruins of the window would
have gone into the street together, only another
girl rushed out from behind a screen and clutched
the falling maiden by the ankles as she lay head-
downwards among the fruit, with her hair flying
loose and a deep blush on her countenance. Then
the customer dropped his umbrella, and fled round
the counter to the rescue, saying, “Aw—permit
me,” but before he had got quite round, the lady
in the window made a tremendous effort and got
out, and lie retired as suddenly as he came. And
what we have been wondering ever since, is
whether he intended to assist to get at the
oranges, or whether he meant to make himself
useful by dragging a complete stranger out of the
window by the ankles. He looked so disappointed
that the latter seemed the more probable. Only
when a pretty girl accidentally stands on her
head in a shop-window, she is sometimes a little
particular about the sort of fellow who lifts her
out.

are already ordering large supplies of gold lace.

Officers of two beche -de - mer boats
arc under arrest at present in Queensland for the
murder of aboriginal members of their crews.
Kidnapping and murder have always been common
to this coastal industry. If the Queensland
Government cannot prevent the like at their own
doors, how on earth arc they to prevent it among
the Kanaka Islands a thousand miles away?
British law lias just decided that a man who
“ hove ” a decanter at a woman’s head and missed
her, breaking a window instead, can’t be punished.
He was charged with “assault and wilful damage
to property,” but he got off on the assault charge
because lie missed the woman—therefore there
was no assault; and ho was acquitted as regards
the wilful damage because ho meant to smash the
female and the smashing of the window was
purely accidental.
Report from an inquest upon a recent
suicide at Liverpool (Eng.): “ Her father stated
that she had a mania for reading sensational
reports, such as murders,, suicides, and other
tragedies. Latterly she had been somewhat
absent-minded. On the night before her death
she sat up with her sister tib 11 o’clock ; then the
sister went to bed, and deceased remained in the
kitchen reading an account of the Rainhill
tragedy. At one o’clock Alice came down and
found her hanging by a shawl from the top of the
kitchen door. There was a difficulty in account-
ing for her position as she was nearly 12 inches off
the ground, and there was no chair or stool at
hand by which she might have climbed up.”
Probably Deeming’* ghost came to her in the
kitchen at midnight, just when the fire was
getting low and the candle had burnt out, and
gave her a lift behind the door so that she could
get her head in the noose. It would have been a
gay spectacle— the spectre on all-fours on the
floor giving a back to the suicide who was too
short to reach up and hang herself without assist-
ance.
Land-buying from the natives in the good
old Maoriland days was profitable, but risky. As
the lands were held on the communal principle, it
was necessary to obtain the signatures of a whole
tribe ere a title-certificate could be secured for the
smallest block. Frequently the fact of one or
two tatooed old chieftains refusing to sign would
cause the purchase of land on which thousands of
pounds had been advanced to remain incomplete
for years—sometimes until the death of the mal-
contents removed the obstacle. Hon, John
Sheehan, a thorough Maori linguist and the first
native-born pakeha to occupy a seat in M.L.
Parliament, was a most successful signature-
getter. His private secretary, a dry, old Scotch
pressman named Garrick, used to tell of his
experiences with Sheehan on a land-buying expe-
dition to the Waikato frontier. “Naethin’but
whusky an’ rum for four blessed days ! No bees-
ness at a’. Hunnerds o’ Moories aboot and a’ o’
them hiecuping ‘ kapai Sheehan ’ all ower the
toonshup. Then Sheehan cam’ to me wi’ a wink
o’ his ee’ an’ says ‘ Noo’s oor time,’ and a’ that day
he kept me twustin’ roon’ a zoetrope—one o’ them
whurligigs wi’ pictures ontil them—to inak’ the
niggers pleasant, and, sirs, he kept singin’ them
‘ The night that Larry was stretched ’ awhiles
whuppin’ cot his parchment an’ gettin’ signatures
like one-o’clock. We pit the whole block through
that day.” Poor Sheehan, who drank himself to
death before lie was 40, was one of the readiest-
witted of men. Struck full on the breast with a
stale egg at a hostile election meeting, he remarked
without a quiver and with a reassuring smile that
almost conveyed the idea that lie accepted the
mal-odorons attention as a compliment, “Gentle-
men, I like a good, strong, healthy opinion, even
when it takes the form of rotten eggs. ’
Cheek is merely a blatant form of honesty,
Fifteen miles of the Cairns (Q.) railway
has cost tlie Queensland Government a million of
money. It, however, opens up a vast extent of-
scenery.
The battle hero, and the fire-brigade hero
and the burning-ship hero, and the railway-
pointsman hero have been very kindly dealt with
by the newspapers, and now The Bulletin
hastens to record the heroism of one-armed men
There is a bullock-driver at Port Albert (Vie.)
who had bis left arm amputated at the shoulder
some years back. Ninety-nine men out of a hun-
dred would have sat down and wept and lived on
the wash-tub exertions of their wives; but not
this one. Ho trained himself to wield the huge
bullock-whip with one hand, to hold the yoke
with his shoulder while he fastened the rings
on the team, to tighten his saddle-girths
with his teeth, and to bridle his horse
with ilia chin and the remaining arm. He can
even fell trees and is one of the best axemen in
the district. He is a tall, gaunt man with tin*
voice of a bo’s’un, and cuts a terribly pathetic
figure in Ins unfortunate mutilation. The other
one-armed hero is a selector at Toora (Vie,),
Many years ago lie lost his left arm through a
mining accident. Since then with the help of his
one arm and the great heart that was more to him
than a dozen arms to the man without grit, h e
has cleared his selection, built him a dwelling-
house and farm buildings, and laid down his
selection with crops. He drove every nail used in
the building of the house, though how he managed
that is a mystery ; he ties and unties knots with
his teeth, rides like a Centaur, and yards cattle
like a Queenslander. Yerily, the heroism of the
one-armed man is the heroism of heroes.
Rev. Father M‘Kernan, the escaped priest,
who promised for a few weeks to be a new Edith
O'Gorman in N.S.W., has left his admirers in a
very tight place. The Father (his front name is
Moike) professed to have seen the error of his
ways, and delivered sundry lectures on the immo-
rality which prevailed in convents, and the way
the unctuous fathers hugged the younger nuns,
and little things like that. He was an attractive
object with the gaslight shining on his tonsure,
and an Orange M.P. in the chair; and the religi-
ous papers took him up with much enthusiasm.
And now, all of a sudden, the reverend Mac.
announces that he can’t stand the horrors of
Protestantism any longer, and has gone
back to the ould faith. He says that no
genuine holy Roman ever 'left the Church,
and he wouldn’t have done it only for the
dhrink, which seems to imply that no man who
isn’t drunk would be a Protestant—therefore all
Protestants are drunk and have to keep so to pre-
vent them falling away from their creed. Further,
Mr. M'Kernan withdraws a statement which he
published in the Protestant Standard that he
had conscientious scruples about Romanism, and
doesn’t seem to think much of the Standard’s
intelligence in printing it. He had hoped, he
said, to get enough money to take him away from
Australia, and as he is still in Australia it would
seem as if Protestants were a mean lot—or else
the reverend gentleman had to drink the takings
to drown his misery. We have received a copy of
Father Moike’s repentance rather badly written
on two sheets of crumpled paper, and witnessed
by a J.P., so we presume the affair is bona-fide.
There is no address upon it—possibly the repentant
father hasn’t an address and is sleeping in the
Domain—but wherever he is it is evident that
Father Mike is going to be a good Roman for the
future. Catholicism, after all, is a disease, and
so is Protestantism, and the person who has got
either of them very bad seldom recovers, unless
the microbes of the complaint are picked out of
him when he is young, and smashed with a big
Bible.
“Thou Shalt Not Bear False Witness.”
One of the divine Commandments directs that
man shall not bear false witness against his
neighbour, and I regret that I should again find it
necessary to warn the public against designing and
dishonest impostors. In the first place “Clements
Tonic” does not mean “a Tonic” made by
“ Clements ” ! “ Clements Tonic ”is the name of
the remedy—it must have a name —but, because
the word Tonic appears, many ignorant people run
off with the erroneous idea that it is merely “a
tonic.” It is time that such fallacies were ex-
plained away. Again I state, Clements Tonic is
not a physic—but a food. It does not create an
appetite for food which the stomach can’t digest,
but, by enriching the blood, it increases the sup-
plies of pancreatic and gastric juices, and thus
enables the stomach to digest more food to supply
the wear and tear or destructive metabolism
which goes on throughout the human body every
moment of our lives from the cradle to the grave.
I want the public to know that the “Dyspeptic”
panaceas advertised with such plausible blarney
are only disguised cathartics, that the temporary
relief is only due to the purging, and not any per-
manent benefit can be derived. These nostrums
are simply commercial speculations, and are pre-
pared by men who never had an hour’s instruction
in, or study of, either pathology, pharmacology,
or therapeutics. Is it any reason because a man
has tried to be a shoemaker and failed that he is
qualified to treat disease ? If he can’t heel slices,
can he heal bodies? Does it stand to common
sense or reason that a man who has never had an
hour’s medical experience knows more about the
human body than men who have devoted their
whole lives and ability to the study of it and its
ailments. These lines are written in the interests
of sufferers and not in mine, it is no loss to me n
you spend your money or put your faith in in-
jurious preparations, and as far as Clements Tonic
is concerned, it stands on its own merits ; it has
grateful patrons in every city, town, and village,
and has lived down all opposition and calumny,
and is now the, standard of excellence and com-
pari-on, and is eulogised and recommended by
faculty, press, and public everywhere, and so long
as this is the case it has no cause to fear the base
misstatements of envious pirates, who one by one
get to Darlinghurst. Verb. Sap,

POLITICAL
POINTS
Rowland Rees, an ex-member of the S.A.
Assembly, whose conceit and oratorical ability are
both phenomenal, went down to listen to the
recent no-confidence motion and slept stretched at
full-length in the Speaker’s gallery, as a sort of
evidence of public contempt for the rhetorical
portion of the performance. , .
During the S.A. no-confldence debate,
Grainger pointed out that ex-Attorney-General
Homburg and ex-Commissioner for Public Works
Jenkins were only naturalised in South Australia,
and that both became aliens directly they crossed
the border of any of the other provinces. Jenkins
is a Yankee, and Homburg a German.
The new S.A. Ministry contains three
Scotchmen, an Irishman, and two Englishmen.
The Premier was an editor, the Chief Secretary is
a doctor, there are two lawyers, a shopkeeper,
and a squatter. Cockburn’s friends think he has
made a great mistake in joining the team, which is
by no means strong, but he put personal loyalty
to Holder above his duty to country and
principle.
The S.A. National Defence League (the
bogus “National” Association under another
name) put forth herculean efforts to keepßlayford
in office, and this induced all the Liberal members
to vote for Holder. Result : Humiliation for the
Ministry and its champions. The N.D.L. boasts
that it will turn local politics inside-out at the
April general election, and much money is being
spent with that object. It is, however, bound to
work “ upon the sly,” for the open advocacy of a
candidate’s cause would be fatal to his chances.
Horn, the wealthiest man in S.A.
Assembly, persuaded Treasurer Rounsvell to float
the recent instalment of Treasury Bills in
Adelaide by guaranteeing the whole issue
(<£1.20,000). He is now very disgusted because the
bills boomed and he was only alloted il!10,000,
while the balance of the money he had got ready
for the use of the State—at 4| per cent, interest-
had to remain unemployed and unprofitable for
several days. He would have voted against the
Government in revenge, but Holder daren't
promise him anything for fear of alienating the
labour members.

Two cable items from Sydney Tele-
graph ;
The idea of Mr. Dibbs seeking to instruct England as t
her fiscal policy, when he has only been in the country
three weeks, is ridiculed by the Economist.
The Economist disputes Mr. Dibbs’ assertion that New
South Wales was compelled to adopt a policy of I loiec-
tion.
Which simply means that the Economist objects
to Mr. Dibbs giving an opinion on English affairs
when he has been in England only three weeks, and
then it straightway proceeds to give an authorita-
tive opinion on Australian affairs though it has
never been in Australia at all. And the Sydney
Telegraph, in its haste to grovel to British
opinion, never notices the humour of the situation.
A few sentences selected from one week s
record in England, as an example of the utter-
brutality of British law —its grovelling reverence
for Property and its callous disregard of human
life and human suffering ;
Sarah Scratton, for stealing four flower-pots from a
nursery-gardener’s pile, one month s imprisonment. A
working-man, for a cowardly assault upon his wife, in
which she received injuries so serious that she had to be
removed to the hospital, also one month. A poor woman,
for stealing a shirt, nine months. (Mrs. Montague, for
slowly murdering her children by persistent cruelty, we
remember, got just three months more, and was “ spared
the indignity ” of prison garb.) A master builder, for a
brutal assault on his old mother, during which lie felled
her to the ground four times and knocked out two of her
teeth—fined £5 ! Jane Close, for stealing a purse con-
taining 4s. 6d., one year’s imprisonment. Peter Coleman
(ao-edlS), who was accused of taking three bricks from a
builder’s yard, 10 days, and five years in a reformaton ;
while a man, of highly respectable appearance, for “re-
volting cruelty” to his horse, fined £l. A ruffian who
showed his annoyance at a speech of his comrade’s by
calmly knocking out his eye, six weeks ; and James
Malliiisou, a collier, for stealing three bottles of spirits,
five years.
Premier Salisbury evidently begins to
realise that Government by Contradiction is a
failure. He says
We go to an election on several issues mixed up. Can
you imagine three trials taking place at once before the
same jury—let us say a trial for murder, a trial for libel,
and a trial of a patent case—and that the jury were only
allowed to say “ Yes” or “ No ” in one verdict, ami that
that verdict was to affect all three cases. That is
exactly the process by which a verdict is to be pronounced
upon the organic institutions of this country.
And that is the way verdicts will be pronounced
till the Referendum is adopted and party govern-
ment is abolished. So long as members go to the
country on a broad platform consisting of (say)
Freetrade, teetotalism, down with the Pope, the
flogging of garrotters, land reform, the extension
of the railway to Possum Flat, republicanism,
black labour, 'the building of a new gaol, and a
general desire to know where Parkes was when
the light went out, the voice of the people will
remain too much mixed up for general compre-
hension.
The A.M.P. Society’s bonus-certificate
form is only about the size of a bank-note, but the
certificates with the accompanying circular issued
for this one year alone weighed just about a ton-
and-a-half.
One of the objectionable things about the
Melbourne Harbour Trust is its association with
Militarism and Navyism. Republicans are vir-
tually excluded, and work is badly done through
the men getting off to all kinds of military
functions.
Martin (Sydney Post-Office) Place is once
more torn up, and, after a few months’enjoyment
of its broad expanse of expensive paving, pedes-
trians have again to jostle along the Post-Office
verandah. Who is responsible for the blunder in
the first place? How many thousands has this
imbecile business cost? Who is going to be
“sacked” over this monstrous waste of public
money ?
Somebody advertises in an English paper
that he “will exchange well-bred fox-terrier for
shower-bath and fittings.” This is another proof
that a man can’t keep himself clean by means of a
fox-terrier.
A copy of the printed proclamation calling
upon all “ loyal ” persons to “beaisy now ” at the

attention in the window of a Oollins-street (Melb.)
tobacconist.
Sundry women in Gallicia have petitioned
the Emperor of Austria for permission to enter
the army, and state that they are as courageous
and as muscular as men, and altogether fitted for
military service. Probably they will get their
wish sooner or later, for the demand for soldiers
in Europe is unlimited. Moreover, a regiment of
hardened widows would be of great value in a
forlorn-hope, especially if they were roused to
madness by a voice from the other side of the
crumbling fortification, telling them that their
bonnets didn’t fit or that their false hair was
coming off. Then that regiment would get into
an irresponsible state, and would rush howling
over every obstruction with its dress bursting
down the'back and its bine spectacles flying in all
directions.
General Booth’s “benevolence.” At the
Salvation Army shelters in London, where the
homeless poor are taken in and cared for by kind
Christian friends in red shirts, this is the system.
If you can pay only a penny you are graciously
permitted to sit up on a form all night. If you
can pay twopence you are allowed to camp in a
sort of egg-box. If yon can't pay anything you
have to do fivepence worth of work at chopping
wood ; then you get one pennyworth of accommo-
dation on the form—the wood is sold for four*
nence —the Army makes threepence profit, and the
business of the regular wood-cutters is ruined by
the Salvation cheap competition. If you have no
money and are not able to chop wood you don’t
get in at all. A small building run on this plan
yields, in one way and another, about 100 per cent,
profit on the money laid out in rent and expenses,
and as the great, dull, imbecile British public
regards this swindle as a “benevolent under-
taking” it sends in its subscriptions into the
bargain—which are all clear profit.
“ Acreocracy ” is a
good word for aristo-
cracy.

Dear Bulletin, —
Your “last-supper” yarn
of Odell, the almost un-
equalled raconteur of
London Savage Club,
who may or may not soon
visit Australia, is but
one of a thousand which
might be told of that
humorist. Some time, age
a well-known bore of the
class which is always
trying to rub shoulders
with well-known Bohe-
mians, was relating to
several men how his
brother, who had, from
his earliest years, given
proof of the possession
of exceptionally brilliant
powers, had unfortu-
nately fallen from a lad-
der, and so injured his
brain as to become almost
an idiot. He kept his
impatient listeners wait-
ing for fully a quarter-
of-an-hour whilst he re-
lated this melancholy in
cident. At last he con-
cluded, whereupon Odell,
who had patiently heard
it all, solemnly chipped
in, with* “ And now, old
fellow, tell us your own
sad story.’’—Yours, L.
London, 12th May, 1892.
A number of Richmond (Aic.) Thrift
electors succeeded in getting their names on the
latest ratepayers’ roll by sending up cheques for
rates due at the last moment. Their names were
inserted, and their cheques subsequently came
back to the local Council marked “ n.s.f. ’ W hich
doesn’t mean “ not sufficient front. - ’
“ The new halfpenny daily paper, The
Morning Leader,” writes our London corres-
pondent by last mail, “ will show oyer the horizon
before the end of this month. Opinions vary as to
the prospects of its ultimate success. It will be a
costly experiment, but the money is said to be all
right, and no expense will be spared in making
it really smart. It will proceed from the Star
office, and as it will no doubt be run on sound
Radical and Republican lines, it will be sure to
‘go,’for there is no thoroughly Radical daily in
London except the Star, and that does not com-
mand the same class of readers which a morning
paper would.”

lady Hopetoun’s manner has been likened

Apparently only one man did a really
big thing out of the Melbourne land-boom —a
young attorney, whom it set up in a landau and
pair, a mansion, and all the appurtenances ; but,
unluckily, he died of tiie joy, like the “Silver
King ” who left Ids money to buy Titans at £4OOO
apiece. The young widow of the young lawyer
has discarded her weeds, and is reckoned the first
prize in Melbourne, as she goes round in the
landau.
# * *
The Earl of Kintore buys his boots in
Sydney, and the man who makes them proudly
exhibits them in the shop-window with “ Lord
Kintore” chalked on the soles. In common
decency the noble lord should buy his hoots where
he draws his salary—in South Australia. Two
policemen could easily get their feet into the canoes
destined to contain the gubernatorial beetle-
crushers. We predict that no Sydney girl will
ever danee with the present Governor of S.A.
after this ruthless exposure of the size of hia
hoofs.
At Adelaide. His Ex. (inspecting a school):
“Now, my good little boy, how many feet make
a yard ?” Good little boy (looking down) : “ One,
sir.”
* * #
A late cousin of the Countess of Kintore
was selling matches in Melbourne before he
assumed an official position in the household of
her husband, the Governor of South Australia.
He was subsequently killed on the railway near
Melbourne while on the razzle-dazzle.
• * *
Mrs. Clara Parker, the missing heroine of
the Joseph (Clarke) adventure, is a comely woman
of —well, let her off at forty. She is of light
complexion, with sandy hair and steel-blue eyes.
She is of average height, with a good figure,
inclining to rotundity. Her husband, a pro-
fessional man, has a nice income, and allowed
her, on their separation, a considerable slice,
which did not satisfy the fascinating grass-widow,
for she sued him for an increase, but lost the case.
She used to keep a stylish boardinghouse in the
city of the Beautiful Harbour.
# * #
Perhaps it is better for poor little Lord
Ancram that he was shot. It would have been a
dreadful fate to live and be matrimonially pursued
by the “ barn-darncers.”
• # •
The news of Lord Ancram’s death, accord-
ing to one more or less bogus “cablegram,” was
received with deep sympathy in the House of
Commons —where probably not one man in ten
had ever heard of Lord Ancram, and not one in
150 had known him intimately.
* * *
The horse which Mr. Edwards, junior, was
riding, when Lord Ancram was shot, is the veteran
outlaw of the station, and is known as “ The
Demon.” It is related that some years ago Mr.
Edwards, sen., narrowly escaped a fate similar to
that of his late guest. While duck shooting a
fellow-sportsman fired unintentionally, and blew
into tatters the hat his host was wearing.

What ! “A Young Lady, well educated;
attractive appearance, with £7OOO, wishes intro-
duction gentleman, permanent situation, from 21
to 40, view matrimony.” Call a hansom and let
us begone—
The Bulletin cannot congratulate the
Sydney daily papers, morning or evening, upon
their occasional illustrations. The literary gourmet
always declares that illustrations, however artistic,
spoil print, and certain it is that they detract
from that indefinable thing, the “dignity” of a
broad-sheet. Especially hideous were Sydney
D.T.’s recent pictures of a steeplechase, and of
Lord Ancram’s funeral, but it has been reserved
for Sydney Evening News to plumb the bottom
of the abyss with its drawing of the house wherein
someone murdered his wife the other day. Out-
side the door were shown two black daubs marked
“ X,” which referred you to the legend attached :
“X, pools of blood.” Why didn’t some mangy
dog lick up those pools of blood in the interests
of journalism ?

ordah everything myself.” Then she turned to

“ Take it with you, ma’am ?” “No / thank you.
Send it /”

# * *
A serious mortality is beginning among
the witnesses in the Deeming case. Sim. Solomon
is dead, and upon his heels another witness -has
started for the shadow-land. This second victim
is Deeraing’s dicky-bird, which was one among
the many items of evidence that led its ovyner to
the gallows. Solomon’s cat has eaten it. All
these things are omens, and now that the trouble
has begun we wouldn’t be Max Hirschfeldt for any-
thing, or if we were Hirschfeldt we would insureour
life for £5OOO right off, and sit down with a sure
and horrible conviction that it would be a paying
investment.
The recent S.A. political crisis might have
given Australia the smallpox scourge. Dr. Toll,
the health officer, was in quarantine, and the
Playford Government had no time to appoint a
temporary successor. Then came the mail steamer,
which was not properly inspected, and smallpox-
infected passengers were allowed to mix with the
population. The possible result cannot yet be
computed.
# * *
A fifteen-year-old girl, charged with steal-
ing jewellery, told Melbourne bench, last week,
that she took the things under orders from “ a
man named Hart,” who had hypnotised her, and
whom she was afraid to disobey. The J.P. com-
plimented her upon the polished aspect of her
mendacity, and let her go upon good-conduct
sureties :
The eight-years’ stretch awarded to Mr,
Abortionist De Wayne at last Sydney Quarter
Sessions was quite a little surprise to that person
and his family circle. His son was sent to ’Frisco
by the last steamer, and pa and ma were to follow
by the next, Mr. Do Wayne having concluded
that Australia isn't a good kind of country for a
free-horn ’Murkan citizen of his kidney to bull-
jack round in. Also, we miss a familiar little
two-line ad. from the medical column of the
evening press.

Miss Larnech, old Donald’s well-gilt only
daughter, is engaged in England to a militia
colonel.
# * *
Bartlett, M.P., complained in the S.A.
Assembly, the other day, that Lord Kintore
delighted to sit at rich men’s tables and to return
their hospitalities, but that the poor farmer and
his wife never got a chance to see the inside of
Government House. Governor Sir Richard
McDonnell, he said, was a homely sort of man
who talked to his cook and watched what she put
in the soup. If tradition be true, Sir Richard
used to have even more tender relations than those
mentioned with some of his domestics.
# • #
Apropos the recent imprisonment of a cer-
tain local preacher for felony, the saint aforesaid
once stood on a platform and said : “My friends,
I have a bi-other who lias taken to drink and
become a victim to the wiles of the Devil, but,
thank Heaven, I have been chastened by the
Almighty, and stand before you a Christian.” A
month ago the saint was lodged in gaol to take
Ids trial, and applied to the drunken brother to
hail him out. “No fear,” said he ; “ such a d
scoundrel can stay in quod. It’ll do him good !”
* * #
Miss Lacy, who battled with the elements
for thirty-six hours after the doomed Quetta went
down, is still, we regret to hear, in a most un-
certain condition of health. The plucky girl’s
mind was virtually gone when the rescue boat
picked her up. For several hours after, she
remained unconscious, while all the time her arms
slowly continued the motion of swimming. She
has never since been able to consecutively recall
her experiences of the catastrophe.
* # *
“Jim" Shaw, boasting of the success of
the ball he gave when Mayor of Adelaide, said
proudly, “Lady Kintore told me it was the best
blanky caper she had ever been at.” The story
got back to Government House and caused much
amusement.
* * *
Mayor Bullock, of Adelaide, gets £lOOO
from the Corporation for his year’s junketings.
Up to now he has spent very little of the amount,
but there is talk of a ball next month. His prede-
cessor, Cohen, laid out ,£5OOO in festivities during
two years of office.

* * *
The few remaining Sydney bill-brokers
are doing a wonderful business. For the big dis-
count companies are so short of cash that it is GO
per cent, with a vengeance, and the Civil-servant
has to eat very humble pie before be can melt his
unearned screw. A Hebrew who, for perhaps 15
years has never re-discounted, took a bundle of
“stiff” to his bank this week rather than lose
good trade.
Tins is the “financier ” whose habit it was in
the “good times” to engage his customer in con-
versation, and, ringing the bell, be would say,
“Abraham, how much did you pay in to-day?”
The clerk would produce a “faked” deposit-slip,
and his master would exclaim, “ Mine Got, only
tree tousan’ pounds ! Why, vat shall I do for
cash ! Mr. Smidt, I should rob mine sbildren if
I gave you more than seventy poun’ for de heel!”

* * #
A verdict of £250 damages has been given
by a Sydney jury to a policeman who bad been
grossly slandered by a railway-official. The latter,
in communicating his slanders to the Inspector-
General of Police, signed himself “ Yours fratern-
ally ” —which seemingly indicated an alleged
fellow-membership of some secret society. It is
satisfactory to note that this in no way availed the
libeller,

retiring from the field. The stock has been

bis creditors and paying them in full, some years
I >ack, was one of the most remembersome things

* * •
Who is watching the interests of the
depositors in the Australian Banking Company?
They must be asleep. Let a meeting be called
and smart, remorseless counsel appointed, and
every penny may yet be recovered.
* # *
In a breach-of-promise case recently tried
in England a verdict was given for £575 damages,
but as the miserable defendant had an income of
only £2 per week he was ordered to pay by
weekly instalments of 7s. Gd., extending over 32
years. Think of the humour of it for that defend-
ant, toiling away from youth to the beginning
of old age, and all for the joy of having once
deliriously hugged a girl in his arms till the whale-
bone creaked again.
# * #
Brisbane Worker on women :
The only practical use which the world has for women
is as mothers. If it were not for that we could get along
without them as readily as we can get along without
Chinese or coolies.
Also, when the case is carefully considered, does
the world really want women as mothers ? It
isn’t such a very gorgeous thing to be born, after
all. We have tried it.
# # #
The other night a swell concert was held
in Sydney. As it was an invitation affair the
audience assembled with praiseworthy punctuality,
and naturally the girl in the cloak-room thought
to gather a rich harvest from the much-bejewelled
Potts Pointers. They came, obtained her assist-
ance in putting themselves straight before enter-
ing, but quite omitted to leave any small coin in
the inviting receptacle provided for the purpose.
In fact, half the audience had departed ere one
lady with some remnants of conscience tendered a
humble shilling. “There!” said the hitherto'
meek damsel, smacking the coin on the table,
“ that’s the first I’ve taken to-night.” ft was ot
no avail, the remaining women decided that it
would only encourage her in impertinence if they
gave her anything for her services.
That girl was not half clever enough for her
business ; she should not have put an empty tray
on the table to invite remuneration, but should
have placed in it a judicious selection of shilling B
and half-crowns, with, perhaps, one modest halt'
sovereign. There’s nothing like a good example.
Young Lady, leave off sighing,
To your empty arms we’re flying
To engage.
We are not too good or naughty,
Too respectable or rorty.
And from twenty-one to forty
Is our age.
Seven thousand “quid ” and kisses!
We have dreamed of luck like this is—
Dreamed of taking such a missis
Off the shelf ;
You will wonder why the deuce you
Pined alone, you little goose, you,
When we come and introduce you
To ourself.
We are strong and highly mettled,
Fresh as roses dewy-petafled,
And on getting nicely settled
We are bent.
As regards our occupation—
Your hymeneal relation
Will be in a situation
Per-ma-nent.
“RULE OF THREE.”
{Suggested by a line in last week's. )
If three men have lost their lives
While building a bank in town,
How many ruined lives
When eight or nine banks “ come down ?”

The gem will rest,
For on her sleeve no heart she’ll wear.

MEAL.
Isn’t it about time that the dismally incon-
gruous inscription—“ John Harris, Mayor,” dis-
appeared from Sydney Centennial Hall ? Harris
is, with the single exception of Sikes, the most -
utterly common and unpoetic name we know, and
the Harris who would put his name in big letters
on such a majestic pile as the Centennial, is in
every respect worthy of the name. In fact, it is
only men with names like Harris who do such
things.

Ellery, Bussell, and Todd will be pleased
to hear that Meteorologist Wragge’s luck is clean
out. They never could stand him. He was too

# # •
Little John F. Paterson, the artist, is off
to London. They gave him a farewell dinner in
Melbourne the other night, with Fink in the
chair (Theo. Fink seems to engage the chair at
these functions), and when the company of 30 had
delivered a speech apiece, and got through 30
toasts, the honoured guest sang his favourite solo,
“We are nae fou,” for the last, last time. Pater-
son and his ballad will be much missed by all who
knew them. “We are nae fou ” had come to be
regarded as an infallible sign that everybody
present was quite fou enough. Henceforth the
artistic world of Melbourne will be apt to get
blind drunk unaware s.
# * #
The residents of Portland (Yic.) have risen
as one man to object to the closing of their gaol.
The Government scheme of retrenchment includes,
the shutting-up of Portland chokee, inasmuch as
there are no local felons for the staff to work upon,
and no indications of a coming crime boom to
relieve the monotony. But rather than see the
shutters up at their skilly-shop, the townspeople
are prepared to receive a draft of “ old and harm-
less prisoners ” from Melbourne and elsewhere,
and the other day a deputation waited upon the
Chief Secretary to ask him whether he couldn’t
send along a consignment of decayed ruffians as
desired. The suggestion was coldly received, how-
ever, so unless Portland is prepared to pay a large
premium for the imported article, and stock the
cells “regardless,” there will shortly be a com-
modious lock-up to let in that peaceful district.

t’other day, poor Jack Something sighfully said :

An awe-inspiring swell rolled up in a
chariot to the best hotel in Newcastle, N.S.W.,
the other day ; was received with marks of rever-
ence, and ushered into the “best spare.” He
aristocratically grumbled at everything, damned
everything, swore at the waiters, and did the lord
generally. Two nights after arrival, he was
“caught red handed'” in a theft. When the
“ swell ” was thus discovered to be only a rough
un, the landlord of “ the best hotel in Newcastle”
who is “particularly wide-awake, don’tcherknow,”
searched the “ swell’s ” Gladstone-bag. Contents ;
. one lump quartz ; one ditto, galena.

The perfection of advertising. One
Henry M'Kenzie sends =£3 to the Argus for th i
benefit of the unemployed, and gets a puff for
himself and his wife, his two sons and his nine
daughters by enclosing the family contribution-
list, which runs as follows :
H. D. M'Kenzie, 10s. ; Mrs. M'Kenzie, 10s. ; Miss bottle
M'Kenzie, ss. ; Miss Lily M'Kenzie, ss. ; Miss Blanche
M'Kenzie, ss. ; Miss Alice M'Kenzie, ss. ; Miss Ruby
M'Kenzie, ss. ; Master Willie M'Kenzie, 2s. (id.; Master
Malcolm M'Kenzie, 2s. 6d. ; Miss Marion M'Kenzie, 2s. Cd.;
Miss Isabel M'Kenzie, 2s. Cd. ; Miss Annie M'Kenzie,
2s. Cd. ; Miss Esther M'Kenzie, 2s. Cd.
Thirteen M'Kenzies altogether trail their sorrow
ful length through the Argus over this one con*
tribution, and the old man also writes a letter
which is published in full, and the editor adds a
footnote bidding the public keep its eye upon the
13 M'Kenzies and do likewise. The M'Kenzie is
evidently a “surgical-joke” Scotchman. It all
reminds us of the Little Boy at Manly.

Smug Pastor Levan, the £3O-a-week man,
came ambling on the platform at Melbourne
Y.M.C.A. meeting the other night. He held
himself up as a sample of the beauty of non-specu-
lation—a sort of Banquo’s Ghost —“ Thou hast no
speculation in the eyes that thou dost glare with.”
But what really sensible man would speculate
while £l5OO a year was coming in, rain or shine?

Detective-Inspector Mackay (Yic.) died
last week His remarkably simple appearance, as
Muling of Beauty(?)
of the fatty fatuous, disguised his undeniable
keenness. It was ho who so effectually investi-
gated that extraordinary affair of the Ferret
steamer, which was run away with by one Hen-
derson, who also stole another man’s wife.
* # *
A kind friend of one of the many newly-
appointed N.S.W. Inspectors of Agriculture says
that that gentleman will be a howling success—
“ so long as he sticks to the task of overseeing the
sowing of wild oats. A matured experience in
that direction should thoroughly qualify him for
the position, and he can proudly point to his per-
sonal success therein, as affording unquestionable
qualifications for the billet.”
# * *
A certain perambulator was very nearly
called into use as an ambulance-cart at some vice-
regal festivities not very long ago. In the middle
of the dance, a prominent barrister made it
apparent to the gay throng that he was not only
beastly, but volubly drunk. Two attenuated
aides managed to seize him just as ho had lost
control of his amorous propensities, and appealed
in a feeble whine to bis sense, of decency—
and then the private sec., arriving suddenly
on the scene, asked, “Who’s this howwid person
and ordered the Law to be indiscriminately
dropped outside on the drive. r lhis was done
with as little display as possible, although the
“ corpse ” sang “ Gawsh shave shqueen, hie,”
violently all the time. Since then, one barrister
knows too well that the Law can be a bass when-
ever it tries, but lie can't make up bis mind to call
at Government House and say so.

Gov,. Jersey is understood to explain that
be divides up the faithful into two sections—Pro-
testant and Catholic, and that as the N.S.W.
Government House dining-room is very small, he
can’t pay court to the sub-sections.
# # *
A patient at Wellington (M.L.) Hospital,
having fallen in love with one of the lady nurses,
didn’t want to leave, so complained of severe
internal pains. The doctors, who had thought him
cured, ordered hot poultices to the parts affected,
and were nonplussed on seeing the report of his
extraordinary temperature. By and by it was
found that he had a trick of warming up the
thermometer with the poultices, and when thus
bowled out he was discharged cured. He now
goes to visit another patient in the same ward.
* # *
Ladies came with baskets of sandwiches to
Melbourne Government Labour Bureau, but were
warned off, wherefore groans. But the fiction
must be kept up that everyone has plenty to eat.
# # #
There is a certain Australian fishery official,
who can repeat the name of every fish, living or
extinct, that is named in Latin, Greek, and some
three or four modern languages, but who cannot
distinguish a yellowtail from a flathead in the
flesh— i.e., in the fish.
# # #
The other day, in Sydney, a Heaven-
canvasser sought a poor footballer who is down
with typhoid. He talked his victim mad in just
an hour-and-a-half. ThFn the demented athlete
leaped out of bed and drop-kicked the foe’s bell-
topper over the Next, lie pitched
the saint’s person the fire-place, then lugged
him forth, formeti a scrum with him over the
battered bat, and scruffed him down, nearly
twisting bis head off in the process. Then he
bustled the corpse under the bed and when help
arrived the tussle had degenerated into a dog-
fight, and the footballer was in the act of chewing
his enemy’s ear.
It was noticed that, a certain austere
Judge, while hearing a breach-of-promise case,
the other day, shifted from the middle of the
bench, where lie usually sits, right up to the
witness-box, close to the pretty gal concerned.
Justice is blind—also a little deaf on that side
probably.

There was quite a crop of Australian pre-
sentees at the last two drawing-rooms, albeit Her
Gracious did not appear in person (says our cor-
respondent). On 16th May, Lady Bray was
presented by Lady Knutsford, who also did the
amiable for Miss Agnes Matheson and Miss
Lydia Reid, whilst Lady Molesworth presented
Mrs. Robert Molesworth. Then Lady Robin-
son presented her grand-daughter, Miss Nea
St. John, who was born in Sydney during Sir
Hercules’ term of office. Mrs. Mourilyan, a niece
of Mr, J. W. Johnson, of Sydney, also made her
obeisance, as did Miss Tooth, daughter of Mr. R.
Lucas Tooth. Miss Tickle was to tiave made her
curtsey also, but at the last moment there w T as a
hitch.
* # #
The love that can survive youth and
beauty is a holy emotion ; you don’t find it often,
but when you do it should be treated with pro-
found respect. The grandest example of it that
we know is a wealthy, battered dame in Sydney—
aged 47, or thereabouts —who nearly every week
travels a hundred miles or so, only to get a
glimpse of an ancient rake who is grey, wrinkled,
toothless, and married. His old flame never
speaks to him on these occasions ; she only looks
at him, and heaves a long sigh, and then goes
home again.
* # #
“ I would not be a director of any company
for a thousand a year,” remarked a well-known
speculator, this week. We know an M.P. who
dreams of Darlinghurst. Yet he is absolutely and
truly guileless. He was a simple guinea-pig, but
cannot retire from his position or the whole pack
of cards would tumble over.

# # *
The Temuka (M.L.) Leader mentions
that a young woman has just taken up a selection
in the Waimate district with her own savings, and
remarks enthusiastically that she is the sort of
female to make a good wife, as she is economical
and “can offer a home to the man who marries
her.” Any homeless young man who has been
deceived and who wants to be taken in out of the
wet would doubtless be glad to meet a girl of this
sort. For our own part, we have known one
female farmer, and that was enough. She used to
get up early in the morning and kill pigs, and she
was a demon at digging post-holes, and for urging
along an exhausted horse in a mud-cart we never
knew her equal. She was a kind-hearted person
all the same, but her muscles were enormous, and
she had a roll in her gait through plodding over
ploughed fields, and when we saw her holding a
horse by the hair with a mighty grasp and blow-
ing medicine down his throat, it made us shudder.
Ultimately she seized upon a lazy, harmless, good-
looking man and bestowed her horny hand upon
him at the altar; she was an affectionate wife,
but she made her husband get up at four in the
morning, and he caught cold in the damp grass
and died after one short year’s happiness. His
widow wept bitterly over his grave, and then
went home and put the old cow’s feet in hot
water. The very last we saw of her she w r as a
powerful old woman of 40, and was shoving a calf
by main force over a fence, while her daughter
(aged 12), who evidently inherited her mother’s
virtues, was scrubbing four rooms and a passage,
and putting the clothes in soak for a day’s wash-
ing. There had been twin daughters originally,
but the other one caught a fatal complaint when
helping to dig potatoes in her eleventh year.
# * #
A witness in a bigamy case at Melbourne
City Court, last week, described himself as a
medium man, who didn’t ill-treat his wife that he
knew of, and always supplied her with sufficient
food to the best of his belief.
# * *
The other morning, from 1 a.m. to 2 a.m.
aN.S. Wales ex-M.P. vociferously harangued a
patient audience of one cabman which had driven
him to the gates of his mansion. At the close of
the orator’s peroration, the Jehu cheered franti-
cally, the horse reared and snorted, and then the
politician, delighted by this enthusiastic recogni-
tion, “parted” a whole fiver.
* * *
Melbourne Y.M.C.A. is in grave financial
difficulties. Goldbug and Giltgore panned out
liberally with the lucre, in boom time, when the
new Y.M.C.A. temple was put up, but their game
is played out, wherefore they draw in their horns.
A miserable exhibition of religion, used as a
stalking horse, to inveigle the wealthy old women
into banks and building societies.
GOOD NEWS FOR VICTORIA.
Be not afraid ! it wasn’t true,
That agitating story—
Great Tulloch still will draw his screw
For Hingland, Home and glory ;
Although his force of men at arms
Be rapidly declining,
He’ll stop and dream of war’s alarm i
He doesn’t mean resigning.
Ho can’t abear to cry “Retrench ’’
And see his braves disbanding ;
He thinks the Russians or the French
Must very soon be landing ;
He scoffs at blank civilian snobs
(Especially when dining),
But though his heart with anger throbs,
He doesn’t mean resigning.
’Tis Tulloch’s biz. to shed the gore
Of all invading strangers,
And though he can’t create a war,
He must exist—on “ dangers,”
So while His Nibs can raise a staff
Though bitterly repining,
You bet your life—it makes us laugh—
He doesn’t mean resigning.
A FANCY.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
When sunset halls are all aglow
With subtle tints divine.
Her face is near me. then I know,
Her hands are clasped in mine.
And all the warmth of sunlit seas,
The glow of ardent skies,
Are cold beside the sympathies
That light her wond’rous eyes.
A soothing peace her presence gives,
A radiant, tranquil rest;
She knows the deep turmoil that lives
Within my troubled breast.
She knows the wounds that bleed and smart,
Her touch, her kiss, is balm,
She whispers, “Oh, thou restless heart,
lie calm awhile, be calm ! ”
She soothes my brow, her gentle palms
Are soft upon my hair—
Oh, then, I stretch my wistful arms,
And—gather empty air.
Brisbane.
K.
A Funny Game.
A few friends met the other night in a Newtown
store to spend a jovial evening, but as they were
all teetotallers and none of them drank anything
stronger than Clements Tonic, drunkenness was
out of the question. Mac. proposed a game
at euchre, and Jones said they had better play for
something just to give the game an interest and
make it lively, but times were hard, so they did
not care to play for money. Smith, however,
spied a box of Fletcher's bilious and liver pills,
and proposed that each time a man was beaten he
should take a pill, and this made the game lively
and the fun was fast and furious. Simpson
swallowed five, Jones got away with four and
Smith and Hopkins two each and Mac. got off
scot free. The game terminated by a general
exodus at 3 a.m., for Dr. Fletcher’s pills never
labour in vain. They are the best and mildest
pills made and are sold by all chemists and
druggists everywhere; being a positive, safe,
and reliable cure for constipation, liver complaint,
headache, &c. They always give satisfaction.
The proprietors give away £5O (in seven prizes) at
Xmas, for the seven first nearest guesses sent in,
as to how many of these pills a large Clements
Tonic bottle (now full) holds. A guess form is
around every box, and all such forms are eligible
for this competition that come from around a box.
One box of Fletcher’s Pills proves their virtues
and they can be obtained everywhere at Is., or
post free 13 penny stamps, from F. M. Clements,
Newtown, N.S.W.

WILLIAM JOHN LYNE.
The census and industrial
RETURNS ACT OF 1891.
NOTICE TO PARTIES REQUIRED TO
MAKE RETURNS.
Under the abovementioned Act returns are re'
quired from the Proprietors or Managers of all
establishments in which steam or other mechanical
power is used, irrespective of the number of hands
employed, and from the Proprietors or Managers
of any establishment in which mechanical power
is not used, but in which five or more persons are
engaged directly or indirectly in working for hire
or reward in any handicraft, or in preparing or
manufacturing articles for trade or sale.
Should any person not falling under the above
description receive forms he should at once return
them to the Government Statistician, with a
written statement of the grounds on which ex-
emption is claimed. Should any person described
above not receive notice, application for forms
should be made without delay to the Government
Statistician, who will at once supply the omission.
All letters, parcels, or packets addressed to the
Government Statistician, if marked with the word
“ Statistics ” on the envelope or wrapper, will be
transmitted through the post free of charge.
(Signed) F. B. SUTTOR.

HOW SHE DOES IT
TO watch a woman despatching her letters by
mail is a highly interesting study. In the
matter of dropping a simple, white, ordinary,
every-day letter, for instance, she affords an
admirable insight into the character of the average
female. Filled with this thought, like as a Labour
Member in parliament with his own importance
we yesterday placed ourselves on duty in a con-
venient spot for observation, near the receipt of
mails. A sedate-looking young lady shortly put
in an appearance. She ' took a letter from a
reticule, which had not been built to accommodate
much more than a society envelope and a postage
stamp, and then suddenly discovered that the
stamp was somewhere else. An exhaustive search
revealed its whereabouts, and having licked it in
two sections and affixed it to the well-wetted right
hand corner of the envelope, she proceeded to-
wards the slot. Her fingers, however, refused to
relax their grip upon the precious message, and
she withdrew it hesitatingly to scan both sides
and be very sure it was securely sealed and
properly addressed, and to be certain that no
inquisitive eye could gleam a hint of its contents
through the cover. Then she “lets it drop,”
which is just what the doctors do when they find
that a case of rheumatism, sciatica, gout, or
neuralgia is beyond their power, and they find
themselves forced to admit that they “can do
nothing more.” But if they can’t, St.” Jacobs Oil
can, a fact which Mr. Risby, of King-street, New-
town, near Sydney, emphasises as follows: “I
have been a crcat sufferer from rheumatism, and
have spent a large sum in doctors’ bills, in addition
to trying nearly every specific I could hear of, but
have never found anything but St. Jac-*-s Oil to
give me relief.”

“German
Syrup”
The majority of well-read phys-
icians now believe that Consump-
tion is a germ disease. In other
words, instead of being in the con-
stitution itself it is caused by innu-
merable small creatures living in the
lungs having no business there and
eating them away as caterpillars do
the leaves of trees.
The phlegm that is
coughed up is those
parts of the lungs
which have been
gnawed off and destroyed. These
little bacilli, as the germs are called,
are too small to be seen with the
naked eye, but they are very much
alive just the same, and enter the
body in our food, in the air we
breathe, and through the pores of
the skin. Thence they get into the
blood and finally arrive at the lungs
where they fasten and increase with
frightful rapidity. Then German
Syrup comes in, loosens them, kills
them, expells them, heals the places
they leave, and so nourish and
sootLfe that, in a short time consump-
tives become germ-proof and well. ®

Disease.

MIMERLESS BREECH-LOADING SHOT GUN. —ONLY 2 POUNDS.
The PEERLESS HAMMERLESS GUN is our latest
offer to the Colonial public and the most marvellous
value we have ever presented for the money.
A RAPID LOADER, CLOSE SHOOTER, HARD
HITTER, and for general utility, equal to guns cost-
ing TEN POUNDS or more. Can only be bought
direct from us, or our authorised agents. Very finely
finished, browned barrel, and walnut stock, with
steel heel-plate, perfect ejectors, and hammer-
less action, lateral breech, no awkward breaking
gear, can be loaded instantaneously, no jamming of
cartridges, never misses or hangs fire.
These guns' are. WITHOUT DOUBT, the
CHEAPEST and BEST ever sold. We have
obtained control of the market, and as the supply is
limited, request customers to order early to avoid
delay and disappointment.
In order to ensure confidence in these guns
we guarantee to refund the money after a trial
If gun proves unsatisfactory, and also to pay
return carriage ourselves.
These guns could never be sold for double the
amount, if it were not that we wish them to be a great
advertisement for us.
THOUSANDS ARE NOW IN USE IN ENGLAND.
Hammerless guns are fast displacing the old style,
for the reason that the absence of hammers is safer
offering no obstacle to catch in bush, &c., to send gun-
off accidentally, and also affording a quicker sight,,
which is very essential in snap shooting.
Specially adapted for Rabbit, Duck, Kangaroo
and Wallaby shooting.
TWELVE BORE ONLY. SINGLE BARREL ONLY.
The cheapest breechloader sold heretofore was at
least double the price of this gun, and we say
positively, much inferior.
The barrel and action of the Peerless gun are of
European manufacture and have formerly been rifle
barrels, and of quite as good quality of metal as the
most expensive gun in use, being manufactured
directly under government supervision, and only
passed after most severe tests.
The boring and chambering have been done with
the one view of hard hitting and close shooting. So
simple and compact is the action, that we assert it to
be almost an impossibility for them to get out of order.

Christchurch,
March 24th., 1892.
Dear Sir, —I am informed that you are about
to visit Sydney and Melbourne^in the interests of
the Mother Mary Joseph Remedies.
I need hardly say that I wish you every success
in your undertaking. At the same time I must
thank you for the samples you kindly sent me.
I must say that I found what I took most
beneficial, and from what I have heard wherever
I have been in New Zealand, these remedies are
looked upon as marvellous in their effects.
I feel sure, when known, they will be equally
appreciated in Australia.
I am,
Yours faithfully,
J. J. Crimes,f

Mr. O. I. Kempthorne.
Mother Mary Joseph Aubert’s name is now a
household one, and the Remedies are being taken
by persons in every sphere of life.
New Zealand can produce as reliable remedies
for sickness and dise'ases as any part of the world,
and we can go further to say that in its vegetation.,
we believe, no country on the face of the globe has
been so bountifully supplied by Nature. Why
then, should the public ask for and use imported
medicines, when they can get them as good, if not
better, in the country which supports them ?
Nature has given us benefits —let us make the
best use of them. The Rev. Mother has just been
on a visit to Wellington, and brought down a
large supply from Jerusalem (on the Wanganui
River), and it has been a pleasure to see this lady’s
face beaming with smiles with the knowledge
that her thirty years’ assiduous experiments,
without fee or reward, have now the appreciation
of the general public. It must indeed be said
that she has proved herself a wonderful woman,,
toiling on for this length of time with the one
motto (“ Excelsior”) always before her. Many a.
fond parent during last month has cause to be
thankful that such a remedy as Marupa lias been
offered, as there has been a very prevalent
epidemic of whooping cough, and its effects on a
large number of children have been simply
marvellous.
Paramo will hold its own as one of the best
Kidney and Liver Cures in existence.
Karana is a tonic not to be equalled anywhere,
whilst Natanata is the best friend on earth to-
suffering females.

Mr. J. M‘lutyre, of Brisbane, the con-
tractor for the supply of telegraph-messengers’
uniforms, writes to po nt out that his price is not
30s. per suit below that of the former contractor
as stated by a correspondent of The Bulletin,
the reduction being a few pence only—that the
youths do not look like “scarecrows or bell-
bottomed larrikins ” —and that the material is
good hnd passed a rigorous inspection before being
accepted. Also he mentions that the supply of
these uniforms has hitherto been a monopoly, and
that this is the first occasion when tenders have
been called for and the business done on a system
of fair and open competition. These statements
are duly endorsed by the Brisbane papers.
The natural beauties of scenery in our
“Beautiful Harbour,” National Park, &e., have
so often been depicted by the artist’s brush as to
be tolerably well-known and appreciated, and
many a fortunate possessor looks with pride at his
pictures of the glorious scenery of sunny New
South Wales. Unfortunately, we are not all
endowed with artistic propensities and cannot use
the brush tj faithfully copy nature: this, how-
ever, in this enlightened age, is only a very slight
drawback, as the wonderful perfection that photo-
graphy has been brought to of late years makes it
a very easy matter to obtain pretty souvenirs of
pleasant journeys. Armed with a small camera,
the tourist can photograph the charming scenes s >
frequent in the numerous excursions around
Sydney, and thus secure lasting impressions of
many a happy day spent ’mid sunshine, flowers,
and ferns. Messrs. Baker & Rouse, of 375 George-
street, Sydney, make it their special duty to
supply the amateur with the necessary apparatus,
and for a ridiculously low price ; they are now :
showing a great variety of cameras, &c., and a
visit to their warehouse will well repay intending
purchasers.

i The secret cipher used in the Twenties and
Ihirties by the British Foreign Office was re-
garded as insoluble without the key. It was
arranged by Jerdan, the journalist, editor of the
long-since-defunct Literary Gazette. He in-
vented it in response to a jocular challenge issued
at a supper-party by Wylde, afterwards Lord
Chancellor. At the party was also present
another young barrister, Wylde’s brother, who
was Judge-Advocate of N.S.W. before the estab-
lishment of the new Charter of Justice. The
latter Wylde obtained a copy of the cipher to
solve, but, failing to do so, put the paper aside.
Years afterwards, during Mr. Commissioner
Bigge’s visit to N.S.W., at a dinner given at
Government House, the conversation turned upon
cryptographs. Macquarie said that Michael
Robinson, his Emancipist clerk, undertook to
decipher any code. Wylde subsequently tested
Robinson’s knowledge on Jordan’s invention, and
the ex-convict supplied a reading which, at a later
day, Wylde submitted to Jerdan, who declared it
coincided with his key. Robinson died without
knowing he had caused a flutter in official circles
at “home.” Robinson used to supply convicts,
for a consideration, with secret codes.
Says a leading Maoriland lawyer :—“ If I
were being tried for a criminal offence and had
my choice, I should ask for a jury if guilty, but if
innocent would prefer a judge alone. No judge,
I think, would ever convict an innocent man, but
a jury often lets a guilty one off. ” In connection
with which view he tells the following to a Bul-
letin correspondent:—“Some years ago, about
14 perhaps, I was engaged to defei d a man
charged with arson. To my mind it v. a? the one
absolutely clearly-proved case of this crime I have
met in my legal career, but I did my best for my
client and pulled the prosecution about as much
as possible. At last came the summing-up, dead
against the prisoner, and the jury, a most ‘con-
victing ’ lot, retired. We expected every minute
to see them return with a verdict of guilty, but
for four hours they remained locked up with-
out deciding. At the end of that time
they filed into Court to ask the judge ‘if
he would kindly go over the crucial points
on which their verdict should rest.’ This
he did, and the points were dead against me, upon
which the jury were about to again retire, when I
jumped to my feet, saying, ‘lf your Honor will
permit, I woidd like to say you have shown the
jury all there was against the prisoner, but nothing
for him.” ‘ TFas there anything for him, Mr. C.?’
blandly replied the judge. ‘Yes,’ I returned,
and, facing the listening twelve, ticked off on my
fingers impressively all I could think of. Five
minutes after the jury came in with a verdict of
‘Not Guilty,’ and the judge was so dumbfounded
he could hardly find breath to discharge the
prisoner, who gaily skipped out of court, and in
less than twenty-four hours was on his way to
Sydney by steamer, not even waiting to try and
get his insurance, which he abandoned.’ ‘ But
your costs?’ exclaimed a listener to the above.
‘Oh ! my costs,’ with a grin, ‘ I got those before I
went into Court.' ”
